### Introduction

Static games are a type of game where players make decisions simultaneously or sequentially, and these decisions are not affected by the decisions of other players. They are often used to model situations where players have incomplete information about each other's preferences or payoffs.

### Subsection: 1.1a Definition of Dominant Strategies

In game theory, a dominant strategy is a strategy that yields the highest payoff for a player, regardless of the strategies chosen by the other players. In other words, a dominant strategy is always the best choice for a player, regardless of the situation. 

Mathematically, a dominant strategy for player $i$ in a game with payoff matrix $A$ is a strategy $s_i$ such that for all other strategies $s_i'$ of player $i$, $A_{s_i,s_i'} \geq A_{s_i',s_i'}$.

Dominant strategies play a crucial role in game theory, as they often lead to Nash equilibria. A Nash equilibrium is a set of strategies where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy. In other words, each player's strategy is the best response to the strategies chosen by the other players.

In the context of static games, dominant strategies can be used to analyze the behavior of players in a game. For example, in a two-player zero-sum game, if both players have a dominant strategy, then the game has a unique Nash equilibrium. However, if one player has a dominant strategy and the other player does not, then the game may have multiple Nash equilibria.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of Nash equilibrium in more detail and discuss how it relates to dominant strategies.




### Subsection: 1.1b Examples of Dominant Strategies

In the previous section, we defined dominant strategies and discussed their importance in game theory. In this section, we will explore some examples of dominant strategies in different types of games.

#### Dominant Strategies in Zero-Sum Games

In zero-sum games, one player's gain is another player's loss. This means that the total payoff for all players is constant, and any change in payoff for one player must be offset by a change in payoff for another player. In these games, dominant strategies are particularly important as they often lead to Nash equilibria.

For example, in the classic game of chess, the dominant strategy for each player is to capture the opponent's king. This strategy is always the best choice for a player, regardless of the opponent's strategy. If both players choose this strategy, the game ends in a draw, which is a Nash equilibrium.

#### Dominant Strategies in Non-Zero-Sum Games

In non-zero-sum games, the total payoff for all players is not constant. This means that the payoff for one player can change without affecting the payoff for another player. In these games, dominant strategies can still be important, but they may not always lead to Nash equilibria.

For example, in the game of "EIMI", the dominant strategy for each player is to choose the option that maximizes their own payoff. However, if all players choose this strategy, the game may not have a unique Nash equilibrium. This is because the payoff for each player depends not only on their own choice, but also on the choices of the other players.

#### Dominant Strategies in Dynamic Games

In dynamic games, players make decisions sequentially, and the decisions of earlier players can affect the decisions of later players. In these games, dominant strategies can be more complex, as they may depend not only on the current state of the game, but also on the expected future behavior of the other players.

For example, in the game of "EIMI", the dominant strategy for each player may change over time, depending on the choices of the other players. This makes it difficult to predict the outcome of the game, and the concept of dominant strategies becomes more nuanced.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of Nash equilibrium in more detail and discuss how it relates to dominant strategies.

### Subsection: 1.2a Definition of Nash Equilibrium

#### Nash Equilibrium in Static Games

In static games, all players make their decisions simultaneously or sequentially, but the decisions are not influenced by the decisions of other players. The Nash equilibrium in these games is a set of strategies where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

For example, in the game of "EIMI", the dominant strategy for each player is to choose the option that maximizes their own payoff. For example, in the game of "Ô ăn quan", a variation of the game "Capablanca chess", the dominant strategy for each player is to choose the move that maximizes their own payoff. Mechanism design is a branch of game theory that deals with the design of mechanisms to achieve desired outcomes in strategic environments. The Nash equilibrium in these games is a set of strategies where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy, taking into account the strategies of other players.

For example, in the game of "Dutch auction", the Nash equilibrium is a set of bids where no player can increase their payoff by unilaterally changing their bid. By understanding the Nash equilibrium, we can design mechanisms that achieve desired outcomes in strategic environments.

#### Existence of Nash Equilibrium

The existence of Nash equilibrium is a fundamental property that ensures the stability of a game. It states that in every game, there exists at least one Nash equilibrium. This property is crucial as it provides a benchmark for predicting the outcome of a game.

For example, in the game of "EIMI", the dominant strategy for each player is to choose the option that maximizes their own payoff. If all players choose this strategy, the game may not have a unique Nash equilibrium. However, the existence of Nash equilibrium ensures that there is at least one stable outcome where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

#### Uniqueness of Nash Equilibrium

The uniqueness of Nash equilibrium is another important property that describes the stability of a game. It states that in a game with a unique Nash equilibrium, no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy. This property is crucial as it provides a clear prediction of the outcome of a game.

#### Robustness of Nash Equilibrium

The robustness of Nash equilibrium is a property that describes the stability of a game in the face of perturbations. This property is crucial as it provides a measure of the resilience of a game to small changes.

For example, in the game of "Dutch auction", the Nash equilibrium is a set of bids where no player can increase their payoff by unilaterally changing their bid. ### Subsection: 1.2c Applications of Nash Equilibrium

In this section, we will explore some of the applications of Nash equilibrium in various fields. Nash equilibrium is a fundamental concept in game theory and has wide-ranging implications in economics, political science, biology, and other disciplines.

#### Applications in Economics

In economics, Nash equilibrium is used to model and analyze various market phenomena. For instance, in the market for a homogeneous good, the equilibrium price and quantity are determined by the point where the demand curve intersects with the supply curve. This point represents a Nash equilibrium, as no player (either the consumer or the producer) can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

In the context of oligopoly, Nash equilibrium is used to model the pricing strategies of firms. Each firm chooses a price that maximizes its profit, taking into account the prices chosen by the other firms. The resulting set of prices is a Nash equilibrium if no firm can increase its profit by unilaterally changing its price.

#### Applications in Political Science

In political science, Nash equilibrium is used to model voting behavior. Each voter chooses a candidate that maximizes their utility, taking into account the choices of the other voters. The resulting set of votes is a Nash equilibrium if no voter can increase their utility by unilaterally changing their vote.

Nash equilibrium is also used to model the behavior of political parties. Each party chooses a policy that maximizes its vote share, taking into account the policies chosen by the other parties. The resulting set of policies is a Nash equilibrium if no party can increase its vote share by unilaterally changing its policy.

#### Applications in Biology

In biology, Nash equilibrium is used to model the evolution of animal behavior. Each animal chooses a behavior that maximizes its fitness, taking into account the behaviors chosen by the other animals. The resulting set of behaviors is a Nash equilibrium if no animal can increase its fitness by unilaterally changing its behavior.

Nash equilibrium is also used to model the evolution of species. Each species chooses a strategy that maximizes its reproductive success, taking into account the strategies chosen by the other species. The resulting set of strategies is a Nash equilibrium if no species can increase its reproductive success by unilaterally changing its strategy.

### Subsection: 1.3a Definition of Subgame Perfect Equilibrium

In the previous sections, we have discussed the concept of Nash equilibrium and its applications in various fields. In this section, we will introduce a stronger form of equilibrium known as the subgame perfect equilibrium.

#### Subgame Perfect Equilibrium

A subgame perfect equilibrium is a solution concept in game theory that extends the concept of Nash equilibrium. It is a state in which no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally deviating from their strategy, taking into account the strategies of the other players in all subgames of the game.

A subgame is a part of a game that can be considered as a separate game in itself. In a game with a tree structure, a subgame is a branch of the tree. In a game with a network structure, a subgame is a connected component of the network.

The concept of subgame perfect equilibrium is particularly useful in games with a tree structure. In these games, the strategies of the players in the subgames can be considered as the strategies in the main game. Therefore, a subgame perfect equilibrium is a state in which no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally deviating from their strategy, taking into account the strategies of the other players in all subgames of the game.

#### Example

Consider the game presented in Figure 1. The game has four subgames, with three proper subgames. The strategies for Player 1 are given by {Up, Uq, Dp, Dq}, whereas Player 2 has the strategies among {TL, TR, BL, BR}.

Using the backward induction, the players will take the following actions for each subgame:

- Subgame 1: Player 1 chooses Up and Player 2 chooses TL.
- Subgame 2: Player 1 chooses Uq and Player 2 chooses TR.
- Subgame 3: Player 1 chooses Dp and Player 2 chooses BL.
- Subgame 4: Player 1 chooses Dq and Player 2 chooses BR.

The resulting state is a subgame perfect equilibrium, as no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally deviating from their strategy. The payoff for Player 1 is (3, 3, 4, 4), and the payoff for Player 2 is (3, 3, 4, 4).

### Subsection: 1.3b Properties of Subgame Perfect Equilibrium

In the previous section, we introduced the concept of subgame perfect equilibrium and provided an example to illustrate its application. In this section, we will delve deeper into the properties of subgame perfect equilibrium and discuss its implications in game theory.

#### Uniqueness

One of the key properties of subgame perfect equilibrium is its uniqueness. In a game with a tree structure, there can be at most one subgame perfect equilibrium. This is because any other state that is not a subgame perfect equilibrium can be improved by unilaterally deviating from the strategy of one of the players, taking into account the strategies of the other players in all subgames of the game. This property is crucial in determining the outcome of a game, as it ensures that there is a unique stable state that all players can reach.

#### Robustness

Another important property of subgame perfect equilibrium is its robustness. A subgame perfect equilibrium is robust to small perturbations in the game. This means that even if the game is slightly modified, the subgame perfect equilibrium remains the same. This property is particularly useful in real-world applications, where games can be complex and subject to various uncertainties.

#### Existence

The existence of a subgame perfect equilibrium is a fundamental question in game theory. In general, the existence of a subgame perfect equilibrium is not guaranteed. However, in certain classes of games, such as congestion games, the existence of a subgame perfect equilibrium can be proven. This is an active area of research, and many efforts are being made to find conditions under which a subgame perfect equilibrium exists.

#### Computation

The computation of a subgame perfect equilibrium is a challenging problem. In general, finding a subgame perfect equilibrium is NP-hard. This means that for large games, it can be computationally infeasible to find a subgame perfect equilibrium. However, for certain classes of games, such as congestion games, efficient algorithms have been developed to compute a subgame perfect equilibrium.

### Subsection: 1.4a Definition of Repeated Games

In the previous sections, we have discussed various types of games, including static games, dynamic games, and extensive-form games. In this section, we will introduce a new type of game known as repeated games.

#### Repeated Games

A repeated game is a type of game that is played over multiple periods. In each period, the players make decisions simultaneously or sequentially, and these decisions are repeated over multiple periods. The key feature of repeated games is that the players remember the past decisions of the other players and can use this information to strategize for future periods.

Repeated games are particularly useful in modeling situations where players interact with each other over multiple periods. For instance, in a market setting, firms may interact with each other over multiple periods, and the decisions made in one period can affect the decisions made in future periods. Similarly, in a political setting, politicians may interact with each other over multiple periods, and the decisions made in one period can affect the reputation of the politicians in future periods.

#### Example

Consider a simple repeated game with two players, A and B, who play over two periods. In each period, the players choose between two strategies, cooperate (C) or defect (D). The payoff matrix for player A is as follows:

| Player A's Payoff Matrix |
|  | C | D |
| C | 3, 3 | 0, 5 |
| D | 5, 0 | 1, 1 |

The payoff matrix for player B is similar, with the roles of player A and player B reversed.

In this game, the players can achieve a higher payoff by cooperating with each other in both periods. However, if one player defects in one period, the other player may defect in the future periods as well, leading to a lower payoff. This is an example of a repeated game with a long-term incentive to cooperate, but a short-term incentive to defect.

This long-term incentive can be seen in the example provided in the previous section, where the players can achieve a payoff of 3, 3 by cooperating in both periods.

However, these long-term incentives can be undermined by short-term incentives. For instance, if one player defects in one period, the other player may defect in the future periods as well, leading to a lower payoff. This short-term incentive to defect can be seen in the example, where player A has a payoff of 5, 0 by defecting in the first period, even though this leads to a lower overall payoff.

Another important property of repeated games is the role of reputation. In a repeated game, the players remember the past decisions of the other players and can use this information to strategize for future periods. This means that a player's reputation can influence the decisions of the other players in future periods.

For instance, in the example provided in the previous section, if player A defects in the first period, player B may infer that player A has a tendency to defect and may defect in the future periods as well. #### Economics

In economics, repeated games are used to model various scenarios such as oligopoly, bargaining, and auctions. In auctions, a repeated game can be used to model the bidding strategies of bidders over multiple periods. The bidders can achieve a higher payoff by cooperating and bidding strategically, but they also have a short-term incentive to defect and bid randomly.

In political science, repeated games are used to model various scenarios such as voting, lobbying, and coalition formation. The voters can achieve a higher payoff by cooperating and voting for the candidate who best represents their interests, but they also have a short-term incentive to defect and vote for a different candidate.

In lobbying, a repeated game can be used to model the interactions between lobbyists and policymakers over multiple periods. The lobbyists can achieve a higher payoff by cooperating and influencing the policymakers to pass legislation that benefits their clients, but they also have a short-term incentive to defect and lobby for different legislation.

In coalition formation, a repeated game can be used to model the interactions between political parties over multiple periods. The parties can achieve a higher payoff by cooperating and forming a coalition government, but they also have a short-term incentive to defect and form a minority government.

#### Biology

In biology, repeated games are used to model various scenarios such as animal behavior, evolution, and disease transmission. For instance, in animal behavior, a repeated game can be used to model the interactions between animals over multiple periods. The animals can achieve a higher payoff by cooperating and behaving in a way that benefits the group, but they also have a short-term incentive to defect and behave in a way that benefits themselves.

In evolution, a repeated game can be used to model the interactions between species over multiple periods. The species can achieve a higher payoff by cooperating and evolving in a way that benefits the group, but they also have a short-term incentive to defect and evolve in a way that benefits themselves.

In disease transmission, a repeated game can be used to model the interactions between infected and susceptible individuals over multiple periods. The individuals can achieve a higher payoff by cooperating and avoiding contact with infected individuals, but they also have a short-term incentive to defect and contact infected individuals.




### Subsection: 1.2b Examples of Nash Equilibrium

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of Nash equilibrium and its application in static and dynamic games. In this section, we will explore some examples of Nash equilibrium in various games.

#### Example 1: The Game of "Ô ăn quan"

The game of "Ô ăn quan" is a variation of the game "Capablanca chess". In this game, two players take turns moving stones between pits on a board. The player who captures the most stones wins. The dominant strategy for each player is to choose the move that maximizes their own payoff. However, the optimal move may depend on the number of players and the current state of the game.

For example, if there are three players, the Nash equilibrium may be for Player 1 to move a stone from pit 1 to pit 2, Player 2 to move a stone from pit 2 to pit 3, and Player 3 to move a stone from pit 3 to pit 1. This strategy results in a payoff of 2 for each player. If Player 1 chooses to move a stone from pit 1 to pit 3, the Nash equilibrium may change to Player 2 moving a stone from pit 2 to pit 3, and Player 3 moving a stone from pit 3 to pit 1. This strategy results in a payoff of 3 for each player.

#### Example 2: The Game of "EIMI"

The game of "EIMI" is a game where two players simultaneously choose one of three options: A, B, or C. The payoff for each player is determined by the combination of options chosen. The dominant strategy for each player is to choose the option that maximizes their own payoff. However, if both players choose the same option, they both receive a payoff of 0.

For example, if both players choose option A, the payoff for each player is 0. If Player 1 chooses option A and Player 2 chooses option B, the payoff for Player 1 is 1 and the payoff for Player 2 is 2. If Player 1 chooses option A and Player 2 chooses option C, the payoff for Player 1 is 3 and the payoff for Player 2 is 4.

In this game, the Nash equilibrium may be for both players to choose option B. This strategy results in a payoff of 2 for each player. If both players choose option A, the Nash equilibrium may change to both players choosing option C. This strategy results in a payoff of 4 for each player.

#### Example 3: The Game of "Ô ăn quan" with Incomplete Information

The game of "Ô ăn quan" can also be played with incomplete information. In this variation, each player has a private signal that determines their payoff for each move. The payoff for each player is determined by the combination of signals and moves.

For example, if both players have the same signal, they both receive a payoff of 0. If Player 1 has signal A and Player 2 has signal B, the payoff for Player 1 is 1 and the payoff for Player 2 is 2. If Player 1 has signal A and Player 2 has signal C, the payoff for Player 1 is 3 and the payoff for Player 2 is 4.

In this game, the Nash equilibrium may be for both players to choose the move that maximizes their own payoff, taking into account the signals of the other player. However, if the signals are not known, the Nash equilibrium may be for both players to choose the move that maximizes their own payoff, assuming that the other player will choose the move that maximizes their own payoff.

### Subsection: 1.2c Application in Game Theory

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of Nash equilibrium and its application in static and dynamic games. In this section, we will explore some specific applications of Nash equilibrium in game theory.

#### Application 1: Market Equilibrium Computation

Nash equilibrium is a fundamental concept in game theory that has been applied to various economic scenarios. One such application is in the computation of market equilibrium. Market equilibrium is a state in which the supply of an item is equal to its demand, resulting in an equilibrium price.

Recently, Gao, Peysakhovich, and Kroer presented an algorithm for online computation of market equilibrium. This algorithm uses the concept of Nash equilibrium to determine the equilibrium price and quantity in a market. The algorithm iteratively updates the price and quantity until it reaches a state where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy. This is the Nash equilibrium.

#### Application 2: Cognitive Hierarchy Theory

Another application of Nash equilibrium is in the field of cognitive hierarchy theory. This theory proposes that players in a game may not always behave rationally, but rather may have different levels of cognitive ability that influence their decision-making process.

The standard solution to the Keynesian Beauty Contest, for example, is determined by iterated elimination of dominated strategies. However, experimental evidence has shown that most players do not behave in accordance with this solution. Instead, they choose numbers around either 25 or 13, which are consistent with first- and second-order depth of reasoning. This supports the cognitive hierarchy theory, which suggests that players may have different levels of cognitive ability that influence their decision-making process.

#### Application 3: Centipede Game

The centipede game is another example of a game where Nash equilibrium has been applied. This game involves two players, Alice and Bob, who take turns moving a token along a line of 100 pennies. The player who moves the token to the end of the line wins all the pennies. The game ends if a player chooses to split the pennies, resulting in a payoff of 25 pennies for each player.

The standard solution to this game is determined by backward induction. However, this solution has been shown to be inconsistent with experimental evidence. Instead, players often choose to split the pennies at different points along the line, resulting in a payoff of 25 pennies for each player. This behavior is consistent with the concept of Nash equilibrium, as it represents a stable state where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

In conclusion, Nash equilibrium is a powerful concept in game theory that has been applied to various economic scenarios. Its applications range from market equilibrium computation to cognitive hierarchy theory and the centipede game. By understanding the concept of Nash equilibrium, we can gain a deeper understanding of strategic decision-making in various economic scenarios.





### Subsection: 1.3a Definition of Mixed Strategies

In the previous sections, we have discussed various concepts such as Nash equilibrium, market equilibrium, and cognitive hierarchy theory. In this section, we will introduce the concept of mixed strategies, which is a fundamental concept in game theory.

#### Introduction to Mixed Strategies

In many games, players may have multiple strategies available to them. For example, in the game of rock-paper-scissors, each player has three strategies: rock, paper, and scissors. However, in some games, players may also have the option to choose a mixed strategy, where they randomly choose between multiple strategies.

A mixed strategy is a probability distribution over the set of pure strategies. For example, in the game of rock-paper-scissors, a player might choose a mixed strategy where they have a 50% chance of choosing rock, a 30% chance of choosing paper, and a 20% chance of choosing scissors.

#### Mixed Strategies in Static Games

In static games, players choose their strategies simultaneously. In these games, mixed strategies can be represented as a vector of probabilities, where each element represents the probability of choosing a particular pure strategy. For example, in the game of rock-paper-scissors, a mixed strategy might be represented as the vector <math>\boldsymbol{\pi} = (0.5, 0.3, 0.2)</math>, where 0.5 represents the probability of choosing rock, 0.3 represents the probability of choosing paper, and 0.2 represents the probability of choosing scissors.

#### Mixed Strategies in Dynamic Games

In dynamic games, players choose their strategies sequentially. In these games, mixed strategies can be represented as a matrix of probabilities, where each row represents the probability of choosing a particular pure strategy for each player. For example, in a two-player game with three strategies each, a mixed strategy might be represented as the matrix <math>\boldsymbol{\Pi} = \begin{bmatrix} 0.5 & 0.3 & 0.2 \\ 0.4 & 0.3 & 0.3 \end{bmatrix}</math>, where the first row represents the mixed strategy for player 1 and the second row represents the mixed strategy for player 2.

#### Mixed Strategies and Nash Equilibrium

In the next section, we will explore the concept of Nash equilibrium in mixed strategies. We will see how mixed strategies can be used to achieve a state of equilibrium in a game, where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

### Subsection: 1.3b Properties of Mixed Strategies

In the previous section, we introduced the concept of mixed strategies and how they can be represented in static and dynamic games. In this section, we will explore some of the key properties of mixed strategies.

#### Probability Distribution

As mentioned earlier, a mixed strategy is a probability distribution over the set of pure strategies. This means that the probabilities of choosing each pure strategy must sum to 1. Mathematically, this can be represented as:

$$
\sum_{i=1}^{n} \pi_i = 1
$$

where <math>\pi_i</math> represents the probability of choosing the <math>i</math>-th pure strategy.

#### Randomization

One of the key properties of mixed strategies is that they allow players to randomize their choices. This means that players can choose to randomly choose between multiple strategies, rather than always choosing the same strategy. This can be particularly useful in games where players have incomplete information about their opponents' strategies.

#### Best Response

Another important property of mixed strategies is that they can be used to find the best response for a player. The best response is the strategy that maximizes a player's payoff, given the strategies of the other players. In the case of mixed strategies, the best response can be found by maximizing the expected payoff. This can be represented as:

$$
\max_{\boldsymbol{\pi}} \sum_{i=1}^{n} \pi_i f_i
$$

where <math>f_i</math> represents the payoff for choosing the <math>i</math>-th pure strategy.

#### Nash Equilibrium

Mixed strategies can also be used to achieve a state of equilibrium in a game. A Nash equilibrium is a state in which no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy. In the case of mixed strategies, a Nash equilibrium can be achieved when no player can increase their expected payoff by changing their mixed strategy. This can be represented as:

$$
\max_{\boldsymbol{\pi}} \sum_{i=1}^{n} \pi_i f_i = \sum_{i=1}^{n} \pi_i f_i
$$

where <math>f_i</math> represents the payoff for choosing the <math>i</math>-th pure strategy.

#### Satisfaction Equilibrium

In some cases, a stronger form of equilibrium known as satisfaction equilibrium can be achieved. A satisfaction equilibrium is a state in which each player is satisfied with their payoff, meaning that they cannot increase their payoff by changing their strategy. This can be represented as:

$$
\max_{\boldsymbol{\pi}} \sum_{i=1}^{n} \pi_i f_i = \sum_{i=1}^{n} \pi_i f_i
$$

where <math>f_i</math> represents the payoff for choosing the <math>i</math>-th pure strategy.

In the next section, we will explore some examples of mixed strategies and how they can be used in various games.

### Subsection: 1.3c Application in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore some applications of mixed strategies in game theory. We will focus on the concept of satisfaction equilibrium, which is a stronger form of equilibrium than Nash equilibrium.

#### Satisfaction Equilibrium in Mixed Strategies

As mentioned in the previous section, a satisfaction equilibrium is a state in which each player is satisfied with their payoff. This means that they cannot increase their payoff by changing their strategy. In the case of mixed strategies, this can be achieved when the expected payoff for each player is equal to their actual payoff. This can be represented as:

$$
\max_{\boldsymbol{\pi}} \sum_{i=1}^{n} \pi_i f_i = \sum_{i=1}^{n} \pi_i f_i
$$

where <math>f_i</math> represents the payoff for choosing the <math>i</math>-th pure strategy.

#### Satisfaction Equilibrium in the Game of Rock-Paper-Scissors

To illustrate the concept of satisfaction equilibrium, let's consider the game of rock-paper-scissors. In this game, each player chooses one of three strategies: rock, paper, or scissors. The payoff matrix for this game is as follows:

| Strategy | Rock | Paper | Scissors |
|---------|------|-------|----------|
| Rock     | 0    | 1     | 2       |
| Paper    | 3    | 0     | 1       |
| Scissors | 2    | 1     | 0       |

In this game, a satisfaction equilibrium can be achieved when each player chooses their strategy with a probability of 1/3. This means that each player is equally likely to choose rock, paper, or scissors. In this case, the expected payoff for each player is equal to their actual payoff, and no player can increase their payoff by changing their strategy.

#### Satisfaction Equilibrium in the Game of Matching Pennies

Another example of a game where satisfaction equilibrium can be achieved is the game of matching pennies. In this game, each player chooses either a head or a tail. The payoff matrix for this game is as follows:

| Strategy | Head | Tail |
|---------|------|------|
| Head     | 1    | -1   |
| Tail    | -1    | 1    |

In this game, a satisfaction equilibrium can be achieved when each player chooses their strategy with a probability of 1/2. This means that each player is equally likely to choose head or tail. In this case, the expected payoff for each player is equal to their actual payoff, and no player can increase their payoff by changing their strategy.

#### Conclusion

In this section, we have explored some applications of mixed strategies in game theory. We have seen how satisfaction equilibrium can be achieved in the games of rock-paper-scissors and matching pennies. In the next section, we will explore some other concepts in game theory, including the concept of a dominant strategy.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fundamentals of static games in microeconomic theory. We have learned about the basic concepts of game theory, including players, strategies, and payoffs. We have also delved into the different types of games, such as zero-sum games, non-zero-sum games, and cooperative games. Additionally, we have discussed the Nash equilibrium and its significance in game theory.

Through the study of static games, we have gained a deeper understanding of how individuals make decisions in situations where their choices affect each other. This knowledge is crucial in various fields, including economics, politics, and psychology. By understanding the principles of game theory, we can better predict and analyze real-world scenarios, leading to more informed decision-making.

As we move forward in our exploration of microeconomic theory, it is important to remember the key concepts and principles learned in this chapter. These concepts will serve as the foundation for more advanced topics, such as dynamic games and mechanism design, which we will cover in the following chapters.

## Chapter: Dynamic Games

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 2: Dynamic Games. In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of dynamic games, a crucial aspect of microeconomic theory. Dynamic games are a type of game theory that deals with decision-making over time, where the decisions of one player can affect the decisions of another player in the future. 

Dynamic games are an essential tool in understanding how economic agents interact and make decisions in a constantly changing environment. They are particularly useful in analyzing situations where the decisions of one player can have a lasting impact on the decisions of another player. 

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of dynamic games, including the role of time, the concept of a strategy path, and the idea of a Nash equilibrium in a dynamic setting. We will also discuss the different types of dynamic games, such as sequential and simultaneous games, and how they can be modeled and solved.

We will also delve into the applications of dynamic games in various fields, such as economics, political science, and biology. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the principles of dynamic games and how they can be used to analyze complex economic phenomena.

So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of dynamic games, where we will learn how to model and solve dynamic games, and understand the implications of these games in various fields.




### Subsection: 1.3b Examples of Mixed Strategies

In this subsection, we will explore some examples of mixed strategies in different games. These examples will help us understand the concept of mixed strategies better and how they can be used to gain an advantage in a game.

#### Example 1: Rock-Paper-Scissors

As mentioned earlier, in the game of rock-paper-scissors, each player has three strategies: rock, paper, and scissors. However, they also have the option to choose a mixed strategy. For example, a player might choose a mixed strategy where they have a 50% chance of choosing rock, a 30% chance of choosing paper, and a 20% chance of choosing scissors. This mixed strategy can be represented as the vector <math>\boldsymbol{\pi} = (0.5, 0.3, 0.2)</math>.

#### Example 2: Capablanca Chess

In Capablanca chess, a variation of the game of chess, players have the option to choose a mixed strategy. For example, a player might choose a mixed strategy where they have a 60% chance of moving their queen, a 30% chance of moving their king, and a 10% chance of moving any other piece. This mixed strategy can be represented as the vector <math>\boldsymbol{\pi} = (0.6, 0.3, 0.1, 0)</math>, where 0.6 represents the probability of choosing to move the queen, 0.3 represents the probability of choosing to move the king, 0.1 represents the probability of choosing to move any other piece, and 0 represents the probability of choosing not to move any piece.

#### Example 3: Satisfaction Equilibrium in Mixed Strategies

In the game of satisfaction equilibrium, players have the option to choose a mixed strategy. For example, a player might choose a mixed strategy where they have a 50% chance of choosing to maximize their own satisfaction, a 30% chance of choosing to maximize the satisfaction of all players, and a 20% chance of choosing to maximize the satisfaction of a randomly chosen player. This mixed strategy can be represented as the vector <math>\boldsymbol{\pi} = (0.5, 0.3, 0.2)</math>.

These examples demonstrate how mixed strategies can be used in different games to gain an advantage. In the next section, we will explore the concept of satisfaction equilibrium in mixed strategies in more detail.

Player B has a payoff of 4 if all three players choose the same strategy, a payoff of 3 if two players choose the same strategy, and a payoff of 2 if only one player chooses the same strategy. Player C has a payoff of 5 if all three players choose the same strategy, a payoff of 4 if two players choose the same strategy, and a payoff of 3 if only one player chooses the same strategy. What is the Nash equilibrium for this game?

Player B has a payoff of 4 if they choose strategy 1 and a payoff of 5 if they choose strategy 2. However, player B does not know the payoffs of player A. What is the Nash equilibrium for this game?

## Chapter: Microeconomic Theory II: A Comprehensive Guide to Advanced Game Theory and Mechanism Design

### Introduction

In the previous chapter, we explored the basics of game theory and its applications in microeconomics. We learned about the different types of games, such as zero-sum games, non-zero-sum games, and cooperative games, and how to analyze them using concepts like Nash equilibrium and Pareto efficiency. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the world of game theory and explore the concept of dynamic games.

Dynamic games are a type of game where players make decisions sequentially, taking into account the decisions of previous players. This adds a new layer of complexity to the game, as players must not only consider their own payoffs, but also the payoffs of other players and how their decisions will affect the outcome of the game. We will explore various types of dynamic games, such as simultaneous-move games, sequential-move games, and repeated games, and learn how to analyze them using advanced game theory techniques.

One of the key concepts we will focus on in this chapter is the concept of subgame perfect equilibrium. This is a stronger form of equilibrium than Nash equilibrium, where players not only consider their own payoffs, but also the payoffs of other players in all possible subgames of the game. We will learn how to find subgame perfect equilibria and how they differ from Nash equilibria.

Another important aspect of dynamic games is the role of mechanism design. In many real-world scenarios, players may not have complete information about the game or may have different preferences or beliefs. In these cases, it is important to design mechanisms that incentivize players to reveal their true preferences and behave strategically. We will explore the basics of mechanism design and its applications in dynamic games.

Overall, this chapter will provide a comprehensive guide to advanced game theory and mechanism design, equipping readers with the necessary tools to analyze and solve complex dynamic games. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a deeper understanding of the intricacies of dynamic games and be able to apply these concepts to real-world scenarios.

In the game of rock-paper-scissors, mixed strategies can be used to gain an advantage over an opponent. For example, if a player knows that their opponent tends to choose rock more often, they can choose a mixed strategy where they have a higher probability of choosing paper or scissors. This can help them win more often against their opponent.

#### Example 2: Capablanca Chess

In Capablanca chess, mixed strategies can be used to gain an advantage over an opponent. For example, if a player knows that their opponent tends to move their queen more often, they can choose a mixed strategy where they have a higher probability of moving their king or other pieces. This can help them gain an advantage in the game.

#### Example 3: Satisfaction Equilibrium in Mixed Strategies

In the game of satisfaction equilibrium, mixed strategies can be used to achieve a higher level of satisfaction for all players. For example, if a player knows that their opponent tends to choose to maximize their own satisfaction, they can choose a mixed strategy where they have a higher probability of choosing to maximize the satisfaction of all players. This can help them achieve a higher level of satisfaction in the game.

### Subsection: 1.4a Definition of Best Response Functions

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of mixed strategies and how they can be used to gain an advantage in a game. In this section, we will explore the concept of best response functions, which is a fundamental concept in game theory.

#### Introduction to Best Response Functions

A best response function is a function that maps a player's strategy to their best response against a given set of strategies from the other players. In other words, it determines the strategy that will give a player the highest payoff against a given set of strategies from the other players.

Best response functions are an important concept in game theory as they help us understand the strategic interactions between players in a game. They allow us to determine the optimal strategies for each player and predict the outcome of a game.

#### Examples of Best Response Functions

To better understand best response functions, let's consider some examples.

##### Example 1: Rock-Paper-Scissors

In the game of rock-paper-scissors, the best response function for each player is determined by the strategies of the other players. For example, if Player 1 chooses rock, Player 2's best response would be to choose paper, as it would give them the highest payoff. Similarly, if Player 1 chooses paper, Player 2's best response would be to choose scissors, and if Player 1 chooses scissors, Player 2's best response would be to choose rock.

##### Example 2: Capablanca Chess

In Capablanca chess, the best response function is more complex as there are more strategies and payoffs involved. However, the concept remains the same. For example, if Player 1 chooses to move their queen, Player 2's best response would be to move their king to a safe location, as it would give them the highest payoff. Similarly, if Player 1 chooses to move their king, Player 2's best response would be to move their queen to a safe location, and if Player 1 chooses to move their bishop, Player 2's best response would be to move their queen to a safe location.

##### Example 3: Satisfaction Equilibrium in Mixed Strategies

In the game of satisfaction equilibrium, the best response function is determined by the strategies of the other players and their preferences. For example, if Player 1 chooses to maximize their own satisfaction, Player 2's best response would be to choose to maximize the satisfaction of all players, as it would give them the highest payoff. Similarly, if Player 1 chooses to maximize the satisfaction of all players, Player 2's best response would be to choose to maximize their own satisfaction, and if Player 1 chooses to maximize the satisfaction of a specific group, Player 2's best response would be to choose to maximize the satisfaction of the same group.

#### Conclusion

In this section, we have explored the concept of best response functions and how they are used in game theory. We have seen examples of best response functions in different games and how they can be used to determine the optimal strategies for each player. In the next section, we will discuss the concept of Nash equilibrium, which is closely related to best response functions.





### Subsection: 1.4b Examples of Best Response Functions

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of best response functions and how they can be used to determine the optimal strategies for each player in a game. In this section, we will explore some examples of best response functions in different games.

#### Example 1: Rock-Paper-Scissors

In the game of rock-paper-scissors, the best response function for each player is determined by the strategies of the other players. For example, if Player 1 chooses rock, Player 2's best response would be to choose paper, as it would give them the highest payoff. Similarly, if Player 1 chooses paper, Player 2's best response would be to choose scissors, and if Player 1 chooses scissors, Player 2's best response would be to choose rock.

#### Example 2: Capablanca Chess

In Capablanca chess, the best response function is more complex as there are more strategies and payoffs involved. However, the concept remains the same. For example, if Player 1 chooses to move their queen, Player 2's best response would be to move their king to a safe location, as it would give them the highest payoff. Similarly, if Player 1 chooses to move their king, Player 2's best response would be to move their queen to a safe location, and if Player 1 chooses to move their bishop, Player 2's best response would be to move their knight to a safe location.

#### Example 3: The Simple Function Point method

The Simple Function Point method is a best response function used in software development to determine the optimal number of function points for a given project. The function points are determined by the complexity of the project and the number of users involved. The best response function for this method is determined by the strategies of the other players, such as the project manager and the development team. For example, if the project manager chooses to increase the number of function points, the development team's best response would be to increase the number of users involved, as it would give them the highest payoff.

#### Example 4: The Simple Function Point method

The Simple Function Point method is a best response function used in software development to determine the optimal number of function points for a given project. The function points are determined by the complexity of the project and the number of users involved. The best response function for this method is determined by the strategies of the other players, such as the project manager and the development team. For example, if the project manager chooses to increase the number of function points, the development team's best response would be to increase the number of users involved, as it would give them the highest payoff.





### Subsection: 1.4c Application in Game Theory

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of best response functions and how they can be used to determine the optimal strategies for each player in a game. In this section, we will explore some applications of best response functions in game theory.

#### Application 1: The Simple Function Point method

The Simple Function Point method is a best response function used in software development to determine the optimal number of function points for a given project. The function points are determined by the complexity of the project and the number of users involved. The best response function for this method is determined by the strategies of the other players, such as the project manager and the development team. For example, if the project manager chooses to increase the number of function points, the development team's best response would be to increase the number of users involved in the project. This would give them the highest payoff, as it would increase the complexity of the project and therefore the number of function points.

#### Application 2: Capablanca Chess

In Capablanca chess, the best response function is more complex as there are more strategies and payoffs involved. However, the concept remains the same. For example, if Player 1 chooses to move their queen, Player 2's best response would be to move their king to a safe location, as it would give them the highest payoff. Similarly, if Player 1 chooses to move their king, Player 2's best response would be to move their queen to a safe location, and if Player 1 chooses to move their bishop, Player 2's best response would be to move their knight to a safe location. This best response function can be applied to other variations of Capablanca chess, such as the three or four player variant, where the best response function becomes even more complex.

#### Application 3: The Keynesian Beauty Contest

The Keynesian Beauty Contest is a game where players must guess the number of points on a dart board. The best response function for this game is determined by the strategies of the other players. For example, if Player 1 chooses to guess a high number, Player 2's best response would be to guess a lower number, as it would give them the highest payoff. This best response function can be applied to other variations of the game, such as the one with three or four players, where the best response function becomes even more complex.

In conclusion, best response functions play a crucial role in game theory, as they allow us to determine the optimal strategies for each player in a game. They can be applied to various games, such as the Simple Function Point method, Capablanca chess, and the Keynesian Beauty Contest, making them a valuable tool in understanding and analyzing games. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fundamentals of static games in microeconomic theory. We have learned that static games are a type of game where players make decisions simultaneously and cannot observe the decisions of other players. This type of game is often used to model situations where players have limited information about each other and must make decisions based on their own information.

We have also discussed the concept of a Nash equilibrium, which is a state in a game where no player can improve their outcome by unilaterally changing their strategy. We have seen that in static games, a Nash equilibrium can be found by analyzing the best response functions of each player. This allows us to determine the optimal strategies for each player and predict the outcome of the game.

Furthermore, we have explored different types of static games, including the prisoner's dilemma, the battle of the sexes, and the rock-paper-scissors game. Each of these games has its own unique characteristics and can be used to illustrate different concepts in game theory.

Overall, this chapter has provided a solid foundation for understanding static games and their role in microeconomic theory. By understanding the fundamentals of static games, we can better analyze and predict the behavior of players in real-world situations.

| A | 5 |

| C | 7 |
| D | 8 |

a) Find the Nash equilibrium of this game.
b) What is the best response function for each player?
c) What is the outcome of the game if both players choose the same strategy?

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fundamentals of static games in microeconomic theory. We have learned that static games are a type of game where players make decisions simultaneously and cannot observe the decisions of other players. This type of game is often used to model situations where players have limited information about each other and must make decisions based on their own information.

We have also discussed the concept of a Nash equilibrium, which is a state in a game where no player can improve their outcome by unilaterally changing their strategy. We have seen that in static games, a Nash equilibrium can be found by analyzing the best response functions of each player. This allows us to determine the optimal strategies for each player and predict the outcome of the game.

Furthermore, we have explored different types of static games, including the prisoner's dilemma, the battle of the sexes, and the rock-paper-scissors game. Each of these games has its own unique characteristics and can be used to illustrate different concepts in game theory.

Overall, this chapter has provided a solid foundation for understanding static games and their role in microeconomic theory. By understanding the fundamentals of static games, we can better analyze and predict the behavior of players in real-world situations.

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 2 of "Microeconomic Theory II: A Comprehensive Guide to Advanced Game Theory and Mechanism Design". In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of dynamic games. These are games where the strategies and payoffs of the players change over time, making them more complex and interesting than static games.

Dynamic games are a crucial part of game theory and mechanism design, as they allow us to model and analyze real-world situations where decisions and outcomes are not fixed but evolve over time. They are used in a wide range of fields, from economics and political science to biology and computer science.

In this chapter, we will cover the basic concepts and techniques used in dynamic games. We will start by introducing the concept of a dynamic game and discussing its key features. We will then move on to discuss the different types of dynamic games, including sequential and simultaneous games, and games with discrete and continuous time.

We will also explore the strategies and payoffs in dynamic games, and how they differ from those in static games. We will discuss the concept of a Nash equilibrium in dynamic games, and how it can be used to analyze the outcomes of these games.

Finally, we will look at some applications of dynamic games in various fields, to illustrate the power and versatility of this tool.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of dynamic games and their role in game theory and mechanism design. You will be equipped with the knowledge and skills to analyze and solve a wide range of dynamic games, and to apply these concepts to real-world situations.

So, let's dive into the world of dynamic games and discover the fascinating strategies and outcomes that these games can generate.




### Subsection: 2.1a Definition of Backward Induction

Backward induction is a method used in game theory to solve dynamic games. It is a form of backward reasoning, where the solution to a game is found by working backwards from the final decision point. This method is particularly useful in games where the decisions of the players affect the payoffs of future periods.

#### 2.1a.1 How Backward Induction Works

The process of backward induction involves two main steps: finding the subgame perfect equilibrium and then solving the game. The subgame perfect equilibrium is the equilibrium that would occur if the game were to be played only in the last period. This equilibrium is found by analyzing the payoffs of the players in each period and identifying the strategies that maximize these payoffs.

Once the subgame perfect equilibrium is found, the game is solved by working backwards from the final period. In each period, the strategies of the players are adjusted to reflect the strategies that would be chosen in the subgame perfect equilibrium. This process continues until the initial period is reached.

#### 2.1a.2 Advantages and Limitations of Backward Induction

Backward induction has several advantages. It allows for the analysis of complex games with multiple players and decision points. It also provides a clear and intuitive way to understand the strategies and payoffs of the players in a game.

However, backward induction also has some limitations. It assumes that the players have perfect information about the game and can calculate the subgame perfect equilibrium. In reality, this is often not the case. Additionally, backward induction can be computationally intensive, especially for games with a large number of players and decision points.

#### 2.1a.3 Applications of Backward Induction

Despite its limitations, backward induction has been successfully applied to a wide range of games. It has been used to analyze games in economics, political science, biology, and computer science. For example, it has been used to study bargaining games, auctions, and evolutionary games.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of backward induction in more detail and discuss some specific examples of its application.




### Subsection: 2.1b Examples of Backward Induction

In this section, we will explore some examples of backward induction in dynamic games. These examples will help illustrate the concepts discussed in the previous section and provide a deeper understanding of how backward induction is applied in practice.

#### 2.1b.1 Backward Induction in a Two-Player Game

Consider a two-player game where the players, Alice and Bob, take turns choosing between two options, A and B. The payoffs are as follows:

| Player | Option A | Option B |
|--------|----------|----------|
| Alice | 3        | 2        |
| Bob | 4        | 1        |

The game is played over three periods, with Alice choosing first in each period. The payoffs are discounted at a rate of 10% per period.

Using backward induction, we can find the subgame perfect equilibrium in the last period. Alice's best option is A, which gives her a payoff of 3. Bob's best option is B, which gives him a payoff of 1. This is the subgame perfect equilibrium.

Working backwards from the last period, we can determine the strategies for the previous periods. In the second period, Alice's best option is still A, which gives her a payoff of 3. Bob's best option is now A, which gives him a payoff of 2. In the first period, Alice's best option is A, which gives her a payoff of 3. Bob's best option is now A, which gives him a payoff of 1.

The final payoffs for each player are:

| Player | Period 1 | Period 2 | Period 3 | Total |
|--------|----------|----------|----------|--------|
| Alice | 3        | 3        | 3        | 9      |
| Bob | 1        | 2        | 4        | 7      |

#### 2.1b.2 Backward Induction in a Multi-Player Game

Now, consider a multi-player game where there are three players, Alice, Bob, and Charlie, and they take turns choosing between two options, A and B. The payoffs are as follows:

| Player | Option A | Option B |
|--------|----------|----------|
| Alice | 3        | 2        |
| Bob | 4        | 1        |
| Charlie | 5        | 3        |

The game is played over three periods, with Alice choosing first in each period. The payoffs are discounted at a rate of 10% per period.

Using backward induction, we can find the subgame perfect equilibrium in the last period. Alice's best option is A, which gives her a payoff of 3. Bob's best option is B, which gives him a payoff of 1. Charlie's best option is A, which gives him a payoff of 5. This is the subgame perfect equilibrium.

Working backwards from the last period, we can determine the strategies for the previous periods. In the second period, Alice's best option is still A, which gives her a payoff of 3. Bob's best option is now B, which gives him a payoff of 2. Charlie's best option is now A, which gives him a payoff of 4. In the first period, Alice's best option is A, which gives her a payoff of 3. Bob's best option is now B, which gives him a payoff of 1. Charlie's best option is now A, which gives him a payoff of 3.

The final payoffs for each player are:

| Player | Period 1 | Period 2 | Period 3 | Total |
|--------|----------|----------|----------|--------|
| Alice | 3        | 3        | 3        | 9      |
| Bob | 1        | 2        | 4        | 7      |
| Charlie | 5        | 4        | 5        | 14      |

#### 2.1b.3 Backward Induction in a Dynamic Game

Now, consider a dynamic game where the players, Alice and Bob, take turns choosing between two options, A and B. The payoffs are as follows:

| Player | Option A | Option B |
|--------|----------|----------|
| Alice | 3        | 2        |
| Bob | 4        | 1        |

The game is played over three periods, with Alice choosing first in each period. The payoffs are discounted at a rate of 10% per period. However, in the second period, Alice can choose to play a wildcard option, C, which gives her a payoff of 5.

Using backward induction, we can find the subgame perfect equilibrium in the last period. Alice's best option is A, which gives her a payoff of 3. Bob's best option is B, which gives him a payoff of 1. In the second period, Alice's best option is now C, which gives her a payoff of 5. Bob's best option is now B, which gives him a payoff of 2. In the first period, Alice's best option is A, which gives her a payoff of 3. Bob's best option is now B, which gives him a payoff of 1.

The final payoffs for each player are:

| Player | Period 1 | Period 2 | Period 3 | Total |
|--------|----------|----------|----------|--------|
| Alice | 3        | 5        | 3        | 11      |
| Bob | 1        | 2        | 4        | 7      |

### Conclusion

In this section, we have explored some examples of backward induction in dynamic games. These examples have illustrated how backward induction can be used to find the subgame perfect equilibrium and solve complex games. By working backwards from the final decision point, we can determine the strategies for each player and calculate the final payoffs. This method is particularly useful in games with multiple players and decision points, as it allows us to consider the strategies of all players and their potential payoffs.





### Related Context
```
# Ô ăn quan

## Variant

A variation of this game exists for three or four players # Capablanca chess

## Strategy

H. G # Cognitive hierarchy theory

## Comparison to standard theory and experimental evidence

Theories of behavior often assume that players think strategically, meaning that players will base their actions on the probable decisions of other players in a way that will serve their objectives. However, many games, both real and contrived, do not result in the equilibrium predicted by standard analytic methods.

The standard solution to the Keynesian Beauty Contest is determined by iterated elimination of dominated strategies. Using the example above, a fully rational player will observe that the most the number could be is 50. This player will also predict that the other players know that as well and will behave accordingly, so the maximum feasible number becomes 25. But, again, other players should know that, too. This process repeats indefinitely, and concludes with all players selecting 0, the Nash equilibrium for this game.

This solution is inconsistent with experimental evidence, which finds that most players choose numbers around either 25 or 13. These guesses are consistent with first- and second-order depth of reasoning, supporting CHT. A small proportion of players exhibit depths of reasoning greater than second order. 

The standard solution to the centipede game is determined by backward induction. According to this method, if Bob reaches his final decision, he will prefer to keep a larger share of a smaller pot to the smaller share of a larger pot, so he will end the game instead of expanding the pot. Alice knows that Bob will end the game on his last move, so she decides to end the game one step before then. However, Bob knows that Alice will end early, so he decides to end just before she does. This process repeats until Alice is confronted with her decision on the first round; knowing that Bob will end the game at the first opportunity, she decides to end the game on her first move. This is the subgame perfect equilibrium for the centipede game.

### Subsection: 2.1c Application in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore the application of backward induction in game theory. Backward induction is a powerful tool that can be used to solve a wide range of dynamic games. It allows us to find the optimal strategies for each player in a game, taking into account the strategies of the other players.

#### 2.1c.1 Backward Induction in Dynamic Games

Backward induction is particularly useful in dynamic games, where players make decisions sequentially. In these games, the strategies of the players can depend on the strategies of the other players, making it difficult to find an optimal solution. Backward induction allows us to work backwards from the final decision point, taking into account the strategies of the other players at each step.

#### 2.1c.2 Backward Induction in Repeated Games

Another important application of backward induction is in repeated games. In these games, players interact with each other multiple times, and their strategies can depend on the strategies of the other players in previous rounds. Backward induction allows us to find the optimal strategies for each player, taking into account the strategies of the other players in each round.

#### 2.1c.3 Backward Induction in Asymmetric Games

Backward induction can also be applied to asymmetric games, where the players have different sets of strategies and payoffs. In these games, the strategies of the players can depend on the strategies of the other players, making it difficult to find an optimal solution. Backward induction allows us to work backwards from the final decision point, taking into account the strategies of the other players at each step.

#### 2.1c.4 Backward Induction in Mechanism Design

Backward induction is a key tool in mechanism design, which is the study of designing mechanisms to achieve desired outcomes in strategic environments. In many mechanism design problems, the strategies of the players can depend on the strategies of the other players, making it difficult to find an optimal solution. Backward induction allows us to work backwards from the final decision point, taking into account the strategies of the other players at each step.

In conclusion, backward induction is a powerful tool that can be applied to a wide range of games and problems in game theory. It allows us to find the optimal strategies for each player, taking into account the strategies of the other players. By working backwards from the final decision point, we can find the subgame perfect equilibrium for a given game. 


# Microeconomic Theory II: A Comprehensive Guide to Advanced Game Theory and Mechanism Design":

## Chapter 2: Dynamic Games:




### Introduction to Subgame Perfect Equilibrium

In the previous chapter, we discussed the concept of Nash equilibrium and its applications in various games. However, in many real-world scenarios, the assumptions made in Nash equilibrium may not hold true. This is where the concept of subgame perfect equilibrium comes into play.

Subgame perfect equilibrium is a refinement of Nash equilibrium that takes into account the strategic interactions between players in a game. It is particularly useful in dynamic games, where players make decisions sequentially and can observe the actions of their opponents.

In this section, we will explore the definition of subgame perfect equilibrium and its importance in game theory. We will also discuss how to find subgame perfect equilibria in different types of games.

### Subsection: 2.2a Definition of Subgame Perfect Equilibrium

Subgame perfect equilibrium is a solution concept in game theory that describes a state in which no player can improve their payoff by deviating from their equilibrium strategy. It is a stronger solution concept than Nash equilibrium, as it takes into account the strategic interactions between players.

To understand subgame perfect equilibrium, we first need to define the concept of a subgame. A subgame is a smaller game within a larger game, where the actions of players are restricted to a specific set of nodes. In other words, a subgame is a game within a game.

Now, let's consider a game with multiple subgames. In this case, a subgame perfect equilibrium is a strategy profile where each player's strategy is optimal not only in the current subgame, but also in all future subgames. This means that each player's strategy is not only a best response to the strategies of their opponents in the current subgame, but also takes into account the strategies of their opponents in all future subgames.

To illustrate this concept, let's consider the example shown in Figure 1. In this game, Player 1 has the strategies {Up, Uq, Dp, Dq}, whereas Player 2 has the strategies among {TL, TR, BL, BR}. Using backward induction, we can determine the subgame perfect equilibrium to be {Dp, TL} with a payoff of (3, 3).

Now, let's consider the game shown in Figure 2. In this game, Player 1 has the strategies {A, B}, and Player 2 has the strategies {X, Y}. Using backward induction, we can determine the subgame perfect equilibrium to be {A, X} with a payoff of (3, 4).

We can see that in both examples, the subgame perfect equilibrium takes into account the strategies of players in all future subgames, resulting in a more robust solution than Nash equilibrium.

In the next section, we will discuss how to find subgame perfect equilibria in different types of games.


## Chapter 2: Dynamic Games:

# Subgame perfect equilibrium

## Example

Determining the subgame perfect equilibrium by using backward induction is shown below in Figure 1. Strategies for Player 1 are given by {Up, Uq, Dp, Dq}, whereas Player 2 has the strategies among {TL, TR, BL, BR}. There are 4 subgames in this example, with 3 proper subgames.

Using the backward induction, the players will take the following actions for each subgame:
Thus, the subgame perfect equilibrium is {Dp, TL} with the payoff (3, 3).

An extensive-form game with incomplete information is presented below in Figure 2. Note that the node for Player 1 with actions A and B, and all succeeding actions is a subgame. Player 2's nodes are not a subgame as they are part of the same information set.

The first normal-form game is the normal form representation of the whole extensive-form game. Based on the provided information, (UA, X), (DA, Y), and (DB, Y) are all Nash equilibria for the entire game.

The second normal-form game is the normal form representation of the subgame starting from Player 1's second node with actions A and B. For the second normal-form game, the Nash equilibrium of the subgame is (A, X).

For the entire game Nash equilibria (DA, Y) and (DB, Y) are not subgame perfect equilibria because the move of Player 2 does not constitute a Nash Equilibrium. The Nash equilibrium (UA, X) is subgame perfect because it incorporates the subgame Nash equilibrium (A, X) as part of its strategy.

To solve this game, first find the Nash Equilibria by mutual best response of Subgame 1. Then use backwards induction and plug in (A,X) → (3,4) so that (3,4) become the payoffs for Subgame 2.

The dashed line indicates that player 2 does not know whether player 1 will play A or B in a simultaneous game. 

Player 1 chooses U rather than D because 3 > 2 for Player 1's payoff. The resulting equilibrium is (A, X) → (3,4). 
Thus, the subgame perfect equilibrium through backwards induction is (UA, X) with the payoff (3, 4).

### Introduction to Subgame Perfect Equilibrium

In the previous chapter, we discussed the concept of Nash equilibrium and its applications in various games. However, in many real-world scenarios, the assumptions made in Nash equilibrium may not hold true. This is where the concept of subgame perfect equilibrium comes into play.

Subgame perfect equilibrium is a refinement of Nash equilibrium that takes into account the strategic interactions between players in a game. It is particularly useful in dynamic games, where players make decisions sequentially and can observe the actions of their opponents.

In this section, we will explore the definition of subgame perfect equilibrium and its importance in game theory. We will also discuss how to find subgame perfect equilibria in different types of games.

### Subsection: 2.2a Definition of Subgame Perfect Equilibrium

Subgame perfect equilibrium is a solution concept in game theory that describes a state in which no player can improve their payoff by deviating from their equilibrium strategy. It is a stronger solution concept than Nash equilibrium, as it takes into account the strategic interactions between players.

To understand subgame perfect equilibrium, we first need to define the concept of a subgame. A subgame is a smaller game within a larger game, where the actions of players are restricted to a specific set of nodes. In other words, a subgame is a game within a game.

Now, let's consider a game with multiple subgames. In this case, a subgame perfect equilibrium is a strategy profile where each player's strategy is optimal not only in the current subgame, but also in all future subgames. This means that each player's strategy is not only a best response to the strategies of their opponents in the current subgame, but also takes into account the strategies of their opponents in all future subgames.

To illustrate this concept, let's consider the example shown in Figure 1. In this game, Player 1 has the strategies {Up, Uq, Dp, Dq}, whereas Player 2 has the strategies {TL, TR, BL, BR}. There are 4 subgames in this example, with 3 proper subgames.

Using the backward induction, the players will take the following actions for each subgame:

- Subgame 1: Player 1 chooses Up and Player 2 chooses TL.
- Subgame 2: Player 1 chooses Uq and Player 2 chooses TR.
- Subgame 3: Player 1 chooses Dp and Player 2 chooses BL.
- Subgame 4: Player 1 chooses Dq and Player 2 chooses BR.

Thus, the subgame perfect equilibrium is {Dp, TL} with the payoff (3, 3).

### Subsection: 2.2b Examples of Subgame Perfect Equilibrium

To further understand subgame perfect equilibrium, let's consider another example shown in Figure 2. In this game, Player 1 has the strategies {A, B} and Player 2 has the strategies {X, Y}. There are 2 subgames in this example, with 1 proper subgame.

Using the backward induction, the players will take the following actions for each subgame:

- Subgame 1: Player 1 chooses A and Player 2 chooses X.
- Subgame 2: Player 1 chooses B and Player 2 chooses Y.

Thus, the subgame perfect equilibrium is {A, X} with the payoff (2, 2).

In this example, we can see that the subgame perfect equilibrium is not the same as the Nash equilibrium. The Nash equilibrium for the entire game is {A, Y} with a payoff of (2, 3). However, this strategy is not subgame perfect as Player 2's move of Y does not constitute a Nash equilibrium. The subgame perfect equilibrium takes into account the strategic interactions between players and ensures that no player can improve their payoff by deviating from their equilibrium strategy.

### Subsection: 2.2c Subgame Perfect Equilibrium in Dynamic Games

In dynamic games, players make decisions sequentially and can observe the actions of their opponents. This makes it even more important to consider subgame perfect equilibrium, as players can adjust their strategies based on the actions of their opponents.

To find the subgame perfect equilibrium in a dynamic game, we can use the concept of backward induction. This involves working backwards from the final subgame and determining the optimal strategies for each player. By doing so, we can ensure that each player's strategy is optimal not only in the current subgame, but also in all future subgames.

In conclusion, subgame perfect equilibrium is a crucial concept in game theory that takes into account the strategic interactions between players. It is a stronger solution concept than Nash equilibrium and is particularly useful in dynamic games. By understanding and applying subgame perfect equilibrium, we can gain a deeper understanding of the behavior of players in strategic situations.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the concept of dynamic games and their role in microeconomic theory. We have learned that dynamic games are games where players make decisions sequentially, taking into account the actions of their opponents. This is in contrast to static games, where all players make decisions simultaneously. We have also seen how dynamic games can be represented using extensive form games, where the game is laid out in a tree-like structure.

We have also discussed the concept of subgame perfect equilibrium, which is a solution concept for dynamic games. This concept takes into account the strategic interactions between players and ensures that no player can improve their payoff by deviating from their equilibrium strategy. We have seen how subgame perfect equilibrium can be found using backward induction, where we work backwards from the final decision point to determine the optimal strategies for each player.

Furthermore, we have explored the concept of discounting, which is the idea that the value of a payoff decreases over time. This is an important concept in dynamic games, as it allows us to account for the fact that players may not have perfect information about the game. We have seen how discounting can be incorporated into the concept of subgame perfect equilibrium, and how it can affect the outcome of a dynamic game.

Overall, dynamic games are an important tool in microeconomic theory, as they allow us to model real-world situations where decisions are made sequentially. By understanding the concepts of subgame perfect equilibrium and discounting, we can better analyze and predict the outcomes of these games.
