#### Exercise 1
Consider the following dynamic game with two players, A and B. Player A has the option to invest in a project, while player B has the option to invest in a different project. The payoffs for each player are as follows:

- If both players invest in the same project, they each receive a payoff of 10.
- If both players invest in different projects, they each receive a payoff of 5.
- If only one player invests, they receive a payoff of 8, while the other player receives a payoff of 2.

Find the subgame perfect equilibrium for this game.

#### Exercise 2
Consider the following dynamic game with three players, A, B, and C. Player A has the option to invest in a project, while players B and C have the option to invest in a different project. The payoffs for each player are as follows:

- If all three players invest in the same project, they each receive a payoff of 15.
- If two players invest in the same project and one player invests in a different project, they each receive a payoff of 10.
- If all three players invest in different projects, they each receive a payoff of 5.

Find the subgame perfect equilibrium for this game.

#### Exercise 3
Consider the following dynamic game with two players, A and B. Player A has the option to invest in a project, while player B has the option to invest in a different project. The payoffs for each player are as follows:

- If both players invest in the same project, they each receive a payoff of 10.
- If both players invest in different projects, they each receive a payoff of 5.
- If only one player invests, they receive a payoff of 8, while the other player receives a payoff of 2.

However, player A has the option to discount their payoff by 20%, while player B has the option to discount their payoff by 30%. Find the subgame perfect equilibrium for this game.

- If both players invest in the same project, they each receive a payoff of 10.
- If both players invest in different projects, they each receive a payoff of 5.
- If only one player invests, they receive a payoff of 8, while the other player receives a payoff of 2.

However, player A has the option to discount their payoff by 20%, while player B has the option to discount their payoff by 30%. Additionally, player A has the option to invest in a third project, which has a payoff of 12 for both players if both invest in it. Find the subgame perfect equilibrium for this game.

The concept of mechanism design was first introduced by economist Leonid Hurwicz in the 1970s. It has since become an essential tool for economists and policymakers, as it provides a framework for designing efficient and fair systems. Mechanism design is particularly useful in situations where there are multiple decision-makers with conflicting interests, and where the outcome of a decision depends on the actions of all decision-makers.

In this chapter, we will cover the basic principles of mechanism design, including the revelation principle and the implementation problem. ### Subsection: 2.2c Application in Game Theory

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of subgame perfect equilibrium and how it can be applied to solve dynamic games. In this section, we will explore some specific applications of subgame perfect equilibrium in game theory.

#### 2.2c.1 Subgame Perfect Equilibrium in the Centipede Game

The Centipede Game is a classic example of a dynamic game where subgame perfect equilibrium can be applied. In this game, two players, Alice and Bob, take turns moving a token along a line of 100 spaces. The player who moves the token to the 100th space wins the game. However, if the token reaches the 100th space, both players lose.

Using subgame perfect equilibrium, we can determine the optimal strategies for each player. Alice's optimal strategy is to move the token to the 99th space, knowing that Bob will move it to the 100th space and lose the game. Similarly, Bob's optimal strategy is to move the token to the 98th space, knowing that Alice will move it to the 99th space and lose the game.

#### 2.2c.2 Subgame Perfect Equilibrium in the Keynesian Beauty Contest

The Keynesian Beauty Contest is another dynamic game where subgame perfect equilibrium can be applied. In this game, players are asked to guess the number of points on a hypothetical dart board. The player who guesses the closest number to the actual number wins the game.

Using subgame perfect equilibrium, we can determine the optimal strategies for each player. The optimal strategy for each player is to guess the maximum number of points that the other players can guess. This strategy is based on the assumption that all players are rational and will predict the same strategy for each other.

#### 2.2c.3 Subgame Perfect Equilibrium in the Capablanca Chess Variant

The Capablanca Chess Variant is a variation of the classic game of chess. In this game, the players take turns moving pieces on a board with 100 squares. The player who captures the most pieces wins the game.

Using subgame perfect equilibrium, we can determine the optimal strategies for each player. The optimal strategy for each player is to capture as many pieces as possible, knowing that the other player will also try to capture as many pieces as possible. This strategy is based on the assumption that both players are rational and will try to maximize their own payoff.

#### 2.2c.4 Subgame Perfect Equilibrium in the Ô ăn quan Game

The Ô ăn quan Game is a variation of the classic game of Go. In this game, two players take turns placing stones on a board with 19x19 intersections. The player who captures the most stones wins the game.

Using subgame perfect equilibrium, we can determine the optimal strategies for each player. The optimal strategy for each player is to capture as many stones as possible, knowing that the other player will also try to capture as many stones as possible. This strategy is based on the assumption that both players are rational and will try to maximize their own payoff.

#### 2.2c.5 Subgame Perfect Equilibrium in the Cognitive Hierarchy Theory

The Cognitive Hierarchy Theory is a theory that explains how players make decisions in dynamic games. In this theory, players are assumed to have a certain depth of reasoning, which determines how they make decisions.

Using subgame perfect equilibrium, we can determine the optimal strategies for each player. The optimal strategy for each player is to use their depth of reasoning to make decisions, knowing that the other player will also use their depth of reasoning to make decisions. This strategy is based on the assumption that both players are rational and will try to maximize their own payoff.

### Conclusion

In this section, we have explored some specific applications of subgame perfect equilibrium in game theory. We have seen how this concept can be applied to solve various dynamic games, including the Centipede Game, the Keynesian Beauty Contest, the Capablanca Chess Variant, the Ô ăn quan Game, and the Cognitive Hierarchy Theory. By understanding the optimal strategies for each player, we can determine the subgame perfect equilibrium and predict the outcome of these games. 





### Subsection: 2.3a Definition of Markov Perfect Equilibrium

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of subgame perfect equilibrium and how it can be applied to solve dynamic games. In this section, we will explore another important equilibrium concept in dynamic games - Markov perfect equilibrium.

#### 2.3a.1 Markov Perfect Equilibrium

Markov perfect equilibrium is a solution concept in dynamic games that is closely related to subgame perfect equilibrium. It is named after the Russian mathematician Andrey Markov, who made significant contributions to the field of stochastic processes.

In a dynamic game, a strategy is said to be Markov perfect if it is optimal given the current state of the game, without considering the history of past actions. This means that the strategy does not depend on the sequence of past actions, but only on the current state of the game.

#### 2.3a.2 Markov Perfect Equilibrium in Dynamic Games

In dynamic games, the concept of Markov perfect equilibrium is particularly useful because it allows us to solve the game without having to consider the entire history of past actions. This can be especially helpful in games with a large number of players and a long sequence of actions.

To illustrate this, let's consider the airfare game discussed in the previous section. In this game, both airlines are engaged in a strategic game when setting prices. Each airline has a Markov strategy for setting the ticket price for a certain route. This strategy does not depend on a history of past observations, but only on the current state of the game.

If both airlines follow this strategy exactly, and passengers always choose the cheapest flight, then each airline is playing a best response to the other airline's strategy. This forms a Markov perfect equilibrium.

#### 2.3a.3 Markov Perfect Equilibrium and Subgame Perfect Equilibrium

It is important to note that Markov perfect equilibrium is a stronger solution concept than subgame perfect equilibrium. In a subgame perfect equilibrium, the strategies are optimal not only in the current state of the game, but also in all subgames. In contrast, in a Markov perfect equilibrium, the strategies are only optimal in the current state of the game.

This means that in a Markov perfect equilibrium, the strategies may not be optimal in all subgames, but they are still optimal in the overall game. This can be particularly useful in games where the strategies are complex and difficult to analyze.

In the next section, we will explore some specific applications of Markov perfect equilibrium in game theory.





### Subsection: 2.3b Examples of Markov Perfect Equilibrium

In this section, we will explore some examples of Markov perfect equilibrium in dynamic games. These examples will help us better understand the concept and its applications.

#### 2.3b.1 Markov Perfect Equilibrium in the Airfare Game

As mentioned in the previous section, the airfare game is a prime example of a dynamic game where Markov perfect equilibrium can be applied. In this game, both airlines are engaged in a strategic game when setting prices. Each airline has a Markov strategy for setting the ticket price for a certain route. This strategy does not depend on a history of past observations, but only on the current state of the game.

If both airlines follow this strategy exactly, and passengers always choose the cheapest flight, then each airline is playing a best response to the other airline's strategy. This forms a Markov perfect equilibrium.

#### 2.3b.2 Markov Perfect Equilibrium in the Repeated Prisoner's Dilemma

The repeated prisoner's dilemma is another example of a dynamic game where Markov perfect equilibrium can be applied. In this game, two prisoners are arrested for a crime and are interrogated separately. Each prisoner has the option to cooperate with the police by confessing to the crime, or to remain silent. The payoff matrix for this game is as follows:

| Prisoner 1 | Prisoner 2 |
|-----------|-----------|
| Confess | Confess | (3,3) |
| Confess | Remain Silent | (5,1) |
| Remain Silent | Confess | (1,5) |
| Remain Silent | Remain Silent | (2,2) |

In this game, the best outcome for both prisoners is to remain silent, resulting in a payoff of 2 for each. However, the temptation to confess and potentially get a higher payoff can be strong.

In a Markov perfect equilibrium, each prisoner has a strategy to confess if the other prisoner has confessed in the previous round, and to remain silent otherwise. This strategy is optimal given the current state of the game, without considering the history of past actions.

#### 2.3b.3 Markov Perfect Equilibrium in the Rock-Paper-Scissors Game

The rock-paper-scissors game is a simple example of a dynamic game where Markov perfect equilibrium can be applied. In this game, two players simultaneously choose one of three options: rock, paper, or scissors. Rock beats scissors, scissors beats paper, and paper beats rock.

In a Markov perfect equilibrium, each player has a strategy to choose rock if the other player has chosen paper in the previous round, to choose paper if the other player has chosen scissors, and to choose scissors if the other player has chosen rock. This strategy is optimal given the current state of the game, without considering the history of past actions.

These examples illustrate the concept of Markov perfect equilibrium and its applications in dynamic games. In the next section, we will explore some extensions and variations of this concept.




### Subsection: 2.3c Application in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore the application of Markov perfect equilibrium in game theory. Game theory is a mathematical framework used to analyze decision-making in situations where the outcome of one's choices depends on the choices of others. It is a powerful tool for understanding strategic interactions in various fields, including economics, political science, and biology.

#### 2.3c.1 Application in Dynamic Games

Markov perfect equilibrium is particularly useful in dynamic games, where the strategies of players evolve over time. In these games, the strategies of players can depend not only on the current state of the game, but also on the history of past observations. This makes the analysis of these games more complex, as the strategies of players can change in response to changes in the game state.

One example of a dynamic game where Markov perfect equilibrium is applied is the airfare game. In this game, the strategies of airlines for setting ticket prices evolve over time, and can depend on the history of past observations. By using Markov perfect equilibrium, we can analyze the strategies of airlines and understand how they respond to changes in the game state.

#### 2.3c.2 Application in Repeated Games

Another important application of Markov perfect equilibrium is in repeated games. In these games, players interact with each other multiple times, and their strategies can depend on the history of past interactions. This makes the analysis of these games even more complex, as the strategies of players can change in response to changes in the interaction history.

One example of a repeated game where Markov perfect equilibrium is applied is the repeated prisoner's dilemma. In this game, the strategies of prisoners for confessing or remaining silent can depend on the history of past interactions. By using Markov perfect equilibrium, we can analyze the strategies of prisoners and understand how they respond to changes in the interaction history.

#### 2.3c.3 Application in Other Games

Markov perfect equilibrium is also applied in other games, such as the game of Ô ăn quan and Capablanca chess. In these games, the strategies of players can depend on the current state of the game, and can change in response to changes in the game state. By using Markov perfect equilibrium, we can analyze the strategies of players and understand how they respond to changes in the game state.

In conclusion, Markov perfect equilibrium is a powerful tool for analyzing strategic interactions in various fields. Its application in dynamic games, repeated games, and other games allows us to understand the strategies of players and how they respond to changes in the game state.

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of dynamic games, a crucial aspect of advanced game theory and mechanism design. We have explored the fundamental concepts, strategies, and outcomes of dynamic games, and how they differ from static games. This makes them more complex and strategic than static games, where players make decisions without knowing the actions of their opponents. We have also seen how the concept of a Nash equilibrium can be extended to dynamic games, and how it can be used to predict the outcome of these games.

In conclusion, dynamic games are a crucial aspect of advanced game theory and mechanism design. They provide a more realistic and strategic framework for understanding decision-making in a wide range of situations. By understanding the dynamics of these games, we can gain valuable insights into the behavior of players and the outcomes of these games.

## Chapter: Chapter 3: Mechanism Design

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 3 of "Microeconomic Theory II: A Comprehensive Guide to Advanced Game Theory and Mechanism Design". In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of Mechanism Design, a critical component of advanced game theory. 

Mechanism Design is a mathematical framework used to design rules or mechanisms that can be used to allocate resources among a group of agents. It is a powerful tool that can be used to solve a wide range of problems, from the allocation of scarce resources to the design of auctions and voting systems. 

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of Mechanism Design, including the revelation principle, the implementation problem, and the concept of incentive compatibility. We will also discuss various solution concepts, such as the Vickrey-Clarke-Groves mechanism and the Groves mechanism, and their applications in different scenarios.

We will also delve into the role of Mechanism Design in microeconomic theory. We will explore how Mechanism Design can be used to solve problems of resource allocation, such as the allocation of public goods, the assignment of tasks to agents, and the design of markets.

This chapter will provide you with a comprehensive understanding of Mechanism Design, equipping you with the necessary tools to analyze and design mechanisms in a variety of contexts. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practitioner, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your journey to mastering advanced game theory and Mechanism Design.

So, let's embark on this exciting journey together, exploring the intricacies of Mechanism Design and its applications in microeconomic theory.




### Subsection: 2.4a Definition of Sequential Equilibrium

Sequential equilibrium is a refinement of Nash equilibrium for extensive form games, introduced by David M. Kreps and Robert Wilson. It is a solution concept that takes into account the strategic interactions between players in a game, and the beliefs that players have about each other's strategies. 

#### 2.4a.1 Basic Concepts

Before delving into the definition of sequential equilibrium, let's review some basic concepts. 

- **Extensive form game**: This is a game with a specific structure, where players make decisions sequentially, and the decisions of earlier players can affect the decisions of later players.
- **Strategy**: A strategy is a plan of action for a player in a game. It specifies what the player will do at each point in the game, given the information available to them.
- **Belief**: A belief is a player's assessment of the probability of a particular event occurring, given the information available to them.
- **Assessment**: An assessment is a profile of strategies and beliefs for a game. It specifies not only a strategy for each player, but also a belief for each player.

#### 2.4a.2 Sequential Equilibrium

A sequential equilibrium is a profile of strategies and beliefs that satisfies certain conditions. Informally, an assessment is a perfect Bayesian equilibrium if its strategies are sensible given its beliefs, and its beliefs are confirmed on the outcome path given by its strategies. 

The formal definition of a sequential equilibrium involves the concept of a consistent assessment. A consistent assessment is a limit point of a sequence of totally mixed strategy profiles and associated sensible beliefs, in the above straightforward sense. This requirement ensures that the assessment is robust to small perturbations in beliefs and strategies.

#### 2.4a.3 Relationship to Other Equilibrium Refinements

Sequential equilibrium is a further refinement of subgame perfect equilibrium and even perfect Bayesian equilibrium. It is itself refined by other equilibrium concepts, such as subgame perfect equilibrium and perfect Bayesian equilibrium. These refinements are important in game theory, as they provide more precise predictions of how players will behave in a game.

In the next section, we will explore some applications of sequential equilibrium in game theory.

### Subsection: 2.4b Properties of Sequential Equilibrium

Sequential equilibrium, as a solution concept, has several important properties that make it a useful tool for analyzing dynamic games. These properties are discussed below:

#### 2.4b.1 Existence

The existence of a sequential equilibrium is a key property that ensures the validity of the solution concept. It states that for every game with a finite number of players and a finite number of information sets, there exists at least one sequential equilibrium. This property is crucial as it ensures that we can always find a solution to a game, even if it is not unique.

#### 2.4b.2 Uniqueness

While the existence of a sequential equilibrium is important, the uniqueness of the equilibrium is equally so. Uniqueness of a sequential equilibrium implies that there is only one possible outcome for the game, given the strategies and beliefs of the players. This property is desirable as it simplifies the analysis of the game and provides a clear prediction of the outcome.

#### 2.4b.3 Robustness

The robustness of a sequential equilibrium refers to its stability against small perturbations in the strategies and beliefs of the players. A robust equilibrium is one that remains an equilibrium even when the strategies and beliefs of the players are slightly altered. This property is important as it ensures that the equilibrium is not overly sensitive to small changes in the game.

#### 2.4b.4 Subgame Perfectness

Sequential equilibrium is a refinement of subgame perfect equilibrium. This means that every sequential equilibrium is also a subgame perfect equilibrium. However, the converse is not always true. This property is important as it ensures that the strategies and beliefs of the players are consistent across all subgames of the main game.

#### 2.4b.5 Perfect Bayesianness

Sequential equilibrium is also a refinement of perfect Bayesian equilibrium. This means that every sequential equilibrium is also a perfect Bayesian equilibrium. However, the converse is not always true. This property is important as it ensures that the strategies and beliefs of the players are consistent with Bayesian rationality.

In the next section, we will explore some applications of sequential equilibrium in game theory.

### Subsection: 2.4c Applications in Game Theory

Sequential equilibrium has been applied to a wide range of games in various fields, including economics, political science, and biology. In this section, we will explore some of these applications, focusing on the use of sequential equilibrium in dynamic games.

#### 2.4c.1 Dynamic Games with Asymmetric Information

One of the most common applications of sequential equilibrium is in dynamic games with asymmetric information. These are games where players have different information about the state of the world, and their strategies and beliefs are influenced by this information. Sequential equilibrium provides a framework for analyzing these games, by considering the strategies and beliefs of the players at each point in the game.

For example, consider a dynamic game with two players, a buyer and a seller, who are negotiating the price of a good. The seller knows the true value of the good, while the buyer only knows a lower bound on its value. The game is sequential, with the buyer making an offer first, and the seller accepting or rejecting this offer.

Using sequential equilibrium, we can analyze the strategies and beliefs of the players in this game. The buyer's strategy might be to start with a low offer, and then increase it if the seller rejects the offer. The seller's strategy might be to accept the first offer that is above their cost. The beliefs of the players might be based on their knowledge of the good's value and the other player's behavior.

#### 2.4c.2 Dynamic Games with Incomplete Information

Sequential equilibrium is also used in dynamic games with incomplete information. These are games where players do not know the strategies and beliefs of the other players, and they must infer this information from their actions. Sequential equilibrium provides a way to analyze these games, by considering the strategies and beliefs of the players at each point in the game, given their incomplete information.

For example, consider a dynamic game with three players, two of whom are cooperating to maximize their joint payoff, while the third player is a potential defector. The game is sequential, with the cooperating players making a joint decision first, and then the defector deciding whether to cooperate or defect.

Using sequential equilibrium, we can analyze the strategies and beliefs of the players in this game. The cooperating players might decide to cooperate if they believe that the defector will also cooperate. The defector might decide to cooperate if they believe that the cooperating players will punish them for defecting. The beliefs of the players might be based on their past interactions and their knowledge of the other players' strategies.

#### 2.4c.3 Dynamic Games with Repeated Interactions

Sequential equilibrium is also used in dynamic games with repeated interactions. These are games where players interact with each other multiple times, and their strategies and beliefs are influenced by their past interactions. Sequential equilibrium provides a framework for analyzing these games, by considering the strategies and beliefs of the players at each point in the game, given their past interactions.

For example, consider a dynamic game with two players who are competing in a market. The players interact with each other multiple times, and their strategies and beliefs are influenced by their past interactions. Using sequential equilibrium, we can analyze the strategies and beliefs of the players in this game. The players might decide to cooperate if they believe that the other player will also cooperate, and they might punish each other if they believe that the other player will defect. The beliefs of the players might be based on their past interactions and their knowledge of the other player's strategies.




#### 2.4b Examples of Sequential Equilibrium

In this section, we will explore some examples of sequential equilibrium to better understand this concept. We will start with a simple example and then move on to more complex ones.

##### Example 1: Rock-Paper-Scissors

Consider the classic game of Rock-Paper-Scissors. In this game, two players simultaneously choose one of three options: rock, paper, or scissors. Rock beats scissors, scissors beats paper, and paper beats rock. 

In the extensive form of this game, the players make their choices sequentially. The first player chooses between rock, paper, or scissors. The second player, knowing the first player's choice, then chooses their own option.

Let's consider a sequential equilibrium for this game. Suppose the first player chooses rock with probability 1/3, paper with probability 1/3, and scissors with probability 1/3. The second player, knowing this, chooses rock with probability 1/2, paper with probability 1/2, and scissors with probability 1/2.

This is a sequential equilibrium because the strategies are sensible given the beliefs, and the beliefs are confirmed on the outcome path given by the strategies. The first player's strategy is sensible because it is a mixed strategy, and the second player's strategy is sensible because it is a best-response to the first player's strategy. The beliefs are confirmed because if the first player chooses rock, the second player's best response is to choose rock with probability 1/2, paper with probability 1/2, and scissors with probability 1/2.

##### Example 2: Market Equilibrium Computation

Consider a market with two firms competing in a duopoly. The firms choose prices simultaneously, and the consumers choose between the two firms based on price and quality. The quality of the firms is given by the parameters $a$ and $b$, and the price of the firms is given by $p_1$ and $p_2$.

In the extensive form of this game, the firms make their choices sequentially. The first firm chooses a price, and the second firm, knowing the first firm's price, then chooses their own price.

Let's consider a sequential equilibrium for this game. Suppose the first firm chooses price $p_1$ with probability $1/2$, and price $p_1 + \epsilon$ with probability $1/2$. The second firm, knowing this, chooses price $p_2$ with probability $1/2$, and price $p_2 + \epsilon$ with probability $1/2$.

This is a sequential equilibrium because the strategies are sensible given the beliefs, and the beliefs are confirmed on the outcome path given by the strategies. The first firm's strategy is sensible because it is a mixed strategy, and the second firm's strategy is sensible because it is a best-response to the first firm's strategy. The beliefs are confirmed because if the first firm chooses price $p_1$, the second firm's best response is to choose price $p_2$ with probability $1/2$, and price $p_2 + \epsilon$ with probability $1/2$.

These examples illustrate the concept of sequential equilibrium. In the next section, we will explore some more complex examples and discuss the implications of sequential equilibrium for various games.

#### Example 3: Fair Random Assignment

Consider a scenario where two agents, A and B, have to be assigned to two different tasks, X and Y. The utilities of the agents for the tasks are given by $u_A(X) = 3$, $u_A(Y) = 1$, $u_B(X) = 1$, and $u_B(Y) = 3$. 

In the extensive form of this game, the agents make their choices sequentially. The first agent chooses between tasks X and Y, and the second agent, knowing the first agent's choice, then chooses their own task.

Let's consider a sequential equilibrium for this game. Suppose the first agent chooses task X with probability 1/2, and task Y with probability 1/2. The second agent, knowing this, chooses task X with probability 1/2, and task Y with probability 1/2.

This is a sequential equilibrium because the strategies are sensible given the beliefs, and the beliefs are confirmed on the outcome path given by the strategies. The first agent's strategy is sensible because it is a mixed strategy, and the second agent's strategy is sensible because it is a best-response to the first agent's strategy. The beliefs are confirmed because if the first agent chooses task X, the second agent's best response is to choose task X with probability 1/2, and task Y with probability 1/2.

#### Example 4: Subgame Perfect Equilibrium

Consider a game with two players, A and B, and four subgames. The strategies for player A are given by {Up, Uq, Dp, Dq}, whereas player B has the strategies among {TL, TR, BL, BR}. There are 4 subgames in this example, with 3 proper subgames.

Using the backward induction, the players will take the following actions for each subgame:
Thus, the subgame perfect equilibrium is {Dp, TL} with the payoff (3, 3).

This example illustrates the concept of subgame perfect equilibrium, which is a refinement of the concept of sequential equilibrium. In a subgame perfect equilibrium, the strategies of the players are not only sensible given the beliefs, but also constitute a Nash equilibrium for each subgame. This ensures that the strategies are not only robust to small perturbations in beliefs and strategies, but also constitute a stable equilibrium in each subgame.




#### 2.4c Application in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore some applications of sequential equilibrium in game theory. We will start with a simple application and then move on to more complex ones.

##### Example 1: Market Equilibrium Computation

Consider a market with two firms competing in a duopoly. The firms choose prices simultaneously, and the consumers choose between the two firms based on price and quality. The quality of the firms is given by the parameters $a$ and $b$, and the price of the firms is given by $p_1$ and $p_2$.

In the extensive form of this game, the firms make their choices sequentially. The first firm chooses a price, and then the second firm chooses a price. The consumers then choose between the two firms based on the prices and qualities.

Let's consider a sequential equilibrium for this game. Suppose the first firm chooses price $p_1$ with probability $1/2$, and price $p_2$ with probability $1/2$. The second firm, knowing this, chooses price $p_1$ with probability $1/2$, and price $p_2$ with probability $1/2$.

This is a sequential equilibrium because the strategies are sensible given the beliefs, and the beliefs are confirmed on the outcome path given by the strategies. The first firm's strategy is sensible because it is a mixed strategy, and the second firm's strategy is sensible because it is a best-response to the first firm's strategy. The beliefs are confirmed because if the first firm chooses price $p_1$, the second firm's best response is to choose price $p_1$ with probability $1/2$, and price $p_2$ with probability $1/2$.

##### Example 2: Irrigation Game

Consider the irrigation game, a classic game in game theory. In this game, a group of farmers must decide how to allocate water from a shared source to their individual plots. The amount of water on each plot is determined by the strategies of the farmers.

In the extensive form of this game, the farmers make their choices sequentially. The first farmer chooses a strategy, and then the next farmer chooses a strategy. The strategies are the amounts of water allocated to each plot.

Let's consider a sequential equilibrium for this game. Suppose the first farmer chooses to allocate $1/2$ of the water to their plot, and the second farmer chooses to allocate $1/2$ of the water to their plot.

This is a sequential equilibrium because the strategies are sensible given the beliefs, and the beliefs are confirmed on the outcome path given by the strategies. The first farmer's strategy is sensible because it is a mixed strategy, and the second farmer's strategy is sensible because it is a best-response to the first farmer's strategy. The beliefs are confirmed because if the first farmer chooses to allocate $1/2$ of the water, the second farmer's best response is to allocate $1/2$ of the water.

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of dynamic games, where players make decisions over time and their decisions can affect the future outcomes of the game. We have learned about the different types of dynamic games, including sequential and simultaneous games, and how they can be modeled using game theory. We have also delved into the concept of Nash equilibrium and how it applies to dynamic games.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of timing in decision-making. In dynamic games, players must not only consider their current decisions but also anticipate how their decisions will affect future outcomes. This requires a deep understanding of the game dynamics and the ability to think strategically.

We have also seen how dynamic games can be used to model real-world scenarios, such as pricing strategies in oligopolistic markets and bargaining between firms. By understanding the principles of dynamic games, we can gain insights into the behavior of firms and individuals in these complex situations.

In conclusion, dynamic games provide a powerful tool for analyzing strategic decision-making over time. By understanding the concepts and techniques presented in this chapter, we can gain a deeper understanding of the complex interactions between firms and individuals in the market.

#### Exercise 1
Consider a dynamic game where two firms are competing in a duopoly market. Firm A can choose to either lower its price or maintain its current price, while Firm B can choose to either lower its price or maintain its current price or exit the market. If both firms lower their prices, they will each earn a profit of $100. If both firms maintain their prices, they will each earn a profit of $50. If one firm lowers its price and the other maintains its price, the firm that lowers its price will earn a profit of $150, while the other firm will earn a profit of $0. If one firm lowers its price and the other exits the market, the firm that lowers its price will earn a profit of $200, while the other firm will earn a profit of $0. If both firms exit the market, they will each earn a profit of $0.

a) What is the Nash equilibrium of this game?
b) How would the outcome change if Firm A had the option to exit the market as well?
c) How would the outcome change if the firms could communicate with each other before making their decisions?

Consider a dynamic game where two firms are bargaining over the price of a product. Firm A can choose to either accept the current price offer or reject it, while Firm B can choose to either increase the price offer or decrease it. If both firms accept the price offer, they will each earn a profit of $100. If one firm accepts the price offer and the other rejects it, the firm that accepts the offer will earn a profit of $150, while the other firm will earn a profit of $0. If both firms reject the price offer, they will each earn a profit of $0.

a) What is the Nash equilibrium of this game?
b) How would the outcome change if the firms could communicate with each other before making their decisions?
c) How would the outcome change if the firms could make multiple offers and counteroffers before reaching a final decision?

The consumer can only buy the product once.

a) What is the Nash equilibrium of this game?
b) How would the outcome change if the consumer could buy the product multiple times?
c) How would the outcome change if the consumer could choose to buy the product at a discounted price now or wait and buy it at a higher price later multiple times?

The large investment now costs $100, while the small investment now costs $50. The large investment later costs $150, while the small investment later costs $75. The firm can only invest once.

a) What is the Nash equilibrium of this game?
b) How would the outcome change if the firm could invest multiple times?
c) How would the outcome change if the firm could choose to invest a large amount now or a small amount now, and invest a large amount later or a small amount later multiple times?

### Introduction

In the previous chapter, we explored the fundamentals of game theory and mechanism design, focusing on scenarios where all players have complete information about the game. However, in many real-world situations, this assumption does not hold true. Incomplete information can arise due to various reasons, such as imperfect information about the game, uncertainty about the actions of other players, or limited knowledge about the preferences and strategies of other players.

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of incomplete information, exploring advanced game theory and mechanism design techniques that are used to model and analyze these complex scenarios. We will begin by discussing the concept of incomplete information and its implications for game theory. We will then introduce the key concepts and tools used in the analysis of incomplete information games, such as Bayesian games and mechanism design with private information.

We will also explore various applications of these concepts in economics, politics, and other fields. For instance, we will discuss how incomplete information can be used to model and analyze auctions, voting systems, and bargaining situations. We will also examine how these concepts can be applied to understand strategic behavior in markets, where firms may have private information about their costs or preferences.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of advanced game theory and mechanism design in the context of incomplete information. You will be equipped with the tools and concepts needed to analyze a wide range of complex scenarios where incomplete information plays a crucial role. So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of incomplete information.




#### 3.1a Definition of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium

The Bayesian Nash Equilibrium (BNE) is a fundamental concept in game theory that extends the Nash equilibrium concept to situations where players have incomplete information about the game. It is named after the British mathematician Thomas Bayes, who first formulated the principles of Bayesian statistics.

In a Bayesian game, each player has a private type, which represents their private information about the game. These types are drawn from a type space, which is a set of possible types for each player. The type of a player is not directly observable by other players, but each player has a belief about the types of the other players based on the information they do have.

A Bayesian Nash Equilibrium is a strategy profile that maximizes the expected payoff for each player given their beliefs and given the strategies played by the other players. That is, a strategy profile $\sigma$ is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium if and only if for every player $i$, keeping the strategies of every other player fixed, strategy $\sigma_i$ maximizes the expected payoff of player $i$ according to that player's beliefs.

In the context of a Bayesian game, a strategy is a function that maps a player's type to a strategy. This means that each player chooses a strategy based on their type, and the strategy is a best response to the strategies of the other players. This concept is analogous to the Nash equilibrium in a non-Bayesian game, where a strategy profile is a Nash equilibrium if every strategy in that profile is a best response to every other strategy in the profile.

The Bayesian Nash Equilibrium is a powerful tool for analyzing games with incomplete information. It allows us to understand how players make decisions when they have limited information about the game, and how these decisions can lead to strategic behavior. In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the concept of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium and explore its applications in various scenarios.

#### 3.1b Properties of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium

The Bayesian Nash Equilibrium (BNE) has several key properties that make it a powerful tool for analyzing games with incomplete information. These properties are derived from the fundamental principles of Bayesian statistics and game theory.

1. **Existence:** The BNE always exists in a Bayesian game. This is because each player's strategy is a best response to the strategies of the other players, given their beliefs. Therefore, there always exists a strategy profile that maximizes the expected payoff for each player.

2. **Uniqueness:** In some cases, the BNE may be unique. This means that there is only one strategy profile that maximizes the expected payoff for each player. However, in many cases, the BNE is not unique. This is because each player's strategy is a best response to the strategies of the other players, given their beliefs. Therefore, there may be multiple strategy profiles that maximize the expected payoff for each player.

3. **Robustness:** The BNE is robust to small perturbations in the game. This means that if the game is slightly modified, the BNE will not change significantly. This property is important in real-world applications, where the game may not be perfectly known.

4. **Subgame Perfection:** The BNE is subgame perfect. This means that the BNE is also a Nash equilibrium in every subgame of the Bayesian game. A subgame is a smaller game that is formed by removing some players or some information from the original game. This property is important because it ensures that the BNE is a stable equilibrium that cannot be improved upon by any player.

5. **Bayesian Rationality:** The BNE is consistent with Bayesian rationality. This means that each player's strategy in the BNE is a best response to their beliefs about the other players' types. This property is important because it ensures that the BNE is a rational outcome that maximizes the expected payoff for each player.

In the next section, we will explore how to compute the BNE in various types of Bayesian games.

#### 3.1c Applications of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium

The Bayesian Nash Equilibrium (BNE) has a wide range of applications in various fields, including economics, political science, and biology. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and how the BNE can be used to analyze and understand these complex systems.

1. **Market Equilibrium:** In economics, the BNE is often used to analyze market equilibrium. In a market, buyers and sellers have incomplete information about the preferences and strategies of the other players. The BNE can be used to determine the equilibrium price and quantity in the market, given the beliefs of the buyers and sellers about the market.

2. **Political Voting:** In political science, the BNE is used to model voting behavior. Each voter has incomplete information about the preferences and strategies of the other voters. The BNE can be used to predict the outcome of an election, given the beliefs of the voters about the other voters.

3. **Animal Behavior:** In biology, the BNE is used to model animal behavior. Each animal has incomplete information about the preferences and strategies of the other animals. The BNE can be used to understand the evolution of animal behavior, given the beliefs of the animals about the other animals.

4. **Game Theory:** In game theory, the BNE is used to analyze a wide range of games, including cooperative games, non-cooperative games, and repeated games. The BNE can be used to understand the strategies and payoffs of the players in these games, given the beliefs of the players about the other players.

5. **Mechanism Design:** In mechanism design, the BNE is used to design mechanisms that achieve a desired outcome. The BNE can be used to design mechanisms that maximize social welfare, given the beliefs of the players about the other players.

In the next section, we will explore how to compute the BNE in various types of Bayesian games.




#### 3.1b Examples of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium

In this section, we will explore some examples of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium (BNE) to further understand this concept. We will start with the Sheriff's Dilemma, a classic example in game theory.

##### Sheriff's Dilemma

The Sheriff's Dilemma is a Bayesian game where a sheriff faces an armed suspect. Both must simultaneously decide whether to shoot the other or not. The suspect can either be of type "criminal" or type "civilian". The sheriff has only one type. The suspect knows its type and the Sheriff's type, but the Sheriff does not know the suspect's type. Thus, there is incomplete information (because the suspect has private information), making it a Bayesian game.

The payoff matrix of this game for both players depends on the type of the suspect. If both players are rational and both know that both players are rational and everything that is known by any player is known to be known by every player (i.e., player 1 knows player 2 knows that player 1 is rational), then the strategy profile $\sigma$ is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium if and only if for every player $i$, keeping the strategies of every other player fixed, strategy $\sigma_i$ maximizes the expected payoff of player $i$ according to that player's beliefs.

In the Sheriff's Dilemma, the BNE can be found by considering the strategies of the sheriff and the suspect. The sheriff's strategy can be to shoot or not shoot, while the suspect's strategy can be to shoot or not shoot depending on their type. The BNE can be found by considering the strategies of the sheriff and the suspect, and the probabilities of each type of the suspect.

##### Market Equilibrium Computation

Another example of a Bayesian game is the Market Equilibrium Computation. In this game, a market equilibrium is computed online. This game is a variation of the classic market equilibrium game, where players have incomplete information about the market. The BNE in this game can be found by considering the strategies of the buyers and sellers, and the probabilities of each type of the buyers and sellers.

These examples illustrate the concept of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium and how it can be applied to various games. In the next section, we will explore the concept of mechanism design, another important topic in advanced game theory.

#### 3.1c Applications of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium

In this section, we will explore some applications of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium (BNE) in various fields. We will start with the application of BNE in the field of mechanism design.

##### Mechanism Design

Mechanism design is a field that deals with the design of mechanisms or rules to achieve a desired outcome in a strategic environment. It is often used in situations where a group of agents must collectively make a decision, and each agent has private information about their preferences or types. BNE is a key concept in mechanism design, as it provides a way to analyze the strategic behavior of agents in these situations.

One of the most famous examples of mechanism design is the "Dutch Auction" mechanism, which was used to allocate a scarce resource among a group of agents with different valuations for the resource. The Dutch Auction mechanism is a direct mechanism, meaning that each agent reports their type (valuation) to the mechanism. The BNE in this game can be found by considering the strategies of the agents and the probabilities of each type of the agents.

##### Online Computation of Market Equilibrium

Another application of BNE is in the online computation of market equilibrium. This is a variation of the classic market equilibrium game, where players have incomplete information about the market. The BNE in this game can be found by considering the strategies of the buyers and sellers, and the probabilities of each type of the buyers and sellers.

The online computation of market equilibrium is a challenging problem, as it involves finding the equilibrium in a dynamic environment where the information about the market is constantly changing. BNE provides a way to analyze this problem, by considering the strategies of the buyers and sellers, and the probabilities of each type of the buyers and sellers.

##### Fair Random Assignment

BNE is also used in the field of fair random assignment, which deals with the problem of assigning a set of agents to a set of objects in a fair way. The fairness criterion can be defined in various ways, such as proportional fairness or envy-freeness. BNE can be used to analyze the strategic behavior of the agents in this game, by considering the strategies of the agents and the probabilities of each type of the agents.

In conclusion, BNE is a powerful tool for analyzing strategic behavior in various fields, including mechanism design, online computation of market equilibrium, and fair random assignment. By considering the strategies of the players and the probabilities of each type of the players, BNE provides a way to find the equilibrium in these games.




#### 3.1c Application in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore the application of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium (BNE) in game theory. We will start with the classic example of the Prisoner's Dilemma, a game that illustrates the concept of BNE and its implications.

##### Prisoner's Dilemma

The Prisoner's Dilemma is a classic game in game theory that illustrates the concept of BNE. In this game, two prisoners are interrogated separately and each has the option to either cooperate with the other by remaining silent, or to defect by betraying the other. The payoff matrix for this game is as follows:

| Player 1 | Player 2 |
|---------|---------|
| Cooperate | Cooperate | 3, 3 |
| Cooperate | Defect | 0, 5 |
| Defect | Cooperate | 5, 0 |
| Defect | Defect | 1, 1 |

The BNE in this game is for both players to defect, even though the outcome would be better for both if they both cooperated. This is an example of a Nash equilibrium, as neither player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

##### Application in Market Equilibrium Computation

The concept of BNE is also applied in the Market Equilibrium Computation game. In this game, a market equilibrium is computed online, and players have incomplete information about the market. The BNE in this game can be found by considering the strategies of the buyers and sellers, and the probabilities of each type of player.

The buyers' strategies can be to bid or not bid, while the sellers' strategies can be to ask a high price or a low price. The BNE can be found by considering the strategies of the buyers and sellers, and the probabilities of each type of player. This can be represented by a payoff matrix, where the payoff for each player is determined by their strategy and the strategy of the other player.

In conclusion, the concept of BNE is a powerful tool in game theory, allowing us to analyze situations where players have incomplete information about each other. It has wide-ranging applications, from classic games like the Prisoner's Dilemma to more complex games like the Market Equilibrium Computation.

## Chapter: Chapter 4: Asymmetric Information

### Introduction

In the realm of microeconomic theory, the concept of asymmetric information plays a pivotal role. This chapter, "Asymmetric Information," delves into the intricacies of this concept, exploring its implications and applications in various economic scenarios. 

Asymmetric information refers to the situation where one party in a transaction has more information than the other. This information asymmetry can lead to market failures, as the party with more information may have an incentive to strategically manipulate the market. This can result in inefficiencies, as the party with less information may not be able to fully understand the true value of the good or service being traded.

In this chapter, we will explore the various forms of asymmetric information that can occur in a market, such as adverse selection and moral hazard. We will also discuss the role of signaling in mitigating these issues. 

We will also delve into the applications of asymmetric information in various economic scenarios, such as labor markets, credit markets, and insurance markets. We will explore how the presence of asymmetric information can affect the functioning of these markets, and how various mechanisms can be designed to address these issues.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of asymmetric information, its implications, and its role in microeconomic theory. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the concept of asymmetric information and its importance in economic analysis. 

We will also provide numerous examples and exercises throughout the chapter to help reinforce the concepts and principles discussed. This chapter is designed to be both informative and engaging, providing readers with a deeper understanding of the complex world of microeconomic theory.




#### 3.2a Definition of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) is a solution concept in game theory that describes the outcome of a dynamic game with incomplete information. It is a refinement of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium (BNE) and is used to solve games where players take turns but are unsure of the "type" of their opponent. This is often the case when players do not know their opponent's preferences between individual moves.

A PBE has two components: strategies and beliefs. The strategies represent the actions that players can take, while the beliefs represent the probabilities that players assign to each other's types. These strategies and beliefs must satisfy certain conditions, which we will discuss in detail below.

The first condition is that a PBE must be a Nash equilibrium. This means that no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy. In other words, each player's strategy is the best response to the strategies of the other players.

The second condition is that the beliefs of each player must be Bayesian consistent. This means that each player's beliefs about the other players' types must be updated in a consistent manner based on the observed actions. This is achieved through Bayesian updating, a process that involves updating beliefs in light of new information.

The third condition is that the beliefs of each player must be Bayesian coherent. This means that each player's beliefs about the other players' types must be coherent with their own beliefs about their own type. This is achieved through the concept of Bayesian coherence, which states that a player's beliefs about their own type must be consistent with their beliefs about the other players' types.

In the next section, we will explore the application of PBE in the context of signaling games.

#### 3.2b Properties of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

The Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) has several important properties that make it a powerful tool in game theory. These properties are derived from the conditions that a PBE must satisfy, as discussed in the previous section.

1. **Uniqueness**: Each game with a finite number of players and a finite number of types has at most one PBE. This property is a direct consequence of the fact that a PBE must be a Nash equilibrium. Since a Nash equilibrium is a stable state where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy, there can only be one such state for a given game.

2. **Robustness**: A PBE is robust to small perturbations in the game. This means that even if the game is slightly modified, the PBE will remain the same. This property is important in real-world applications, where games are often subject to small changes over time.

3. **Bayesian Consistency**: As mentioned earlier, a PBE must satisfy the condition of Bayesian consistency. This means that each player's beliefs about the other players' types must be updated in a consistent manner based on the observed actions. This property ensures that players' beliefs are rational and coherent.

4. **Bayesian Coherence**: A PBE must also satisfy the condition of Bayesian coherence. This means that each player's beliefs about their own type must be coherent with their beliefs about the other players' types. This property ensures that players' beliefs are not only rational and coherent, but also consistent across different types.

5. **Subgame Perfection**: A PBE is subgame perfect. This means that it is a Nash equilibrium not only in the entire game, but also in every subgame. A subgame is a smaller game within the larger game, and subgame perfection ensures that the PBE is stable even when the game is broken down into smaller parts.

These properties make the Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium a powerful tool in game theory. It allows us to analyze complex games with incomplete information and find stable outcomes that are robust to small changes in the game. In the next section, we will explore the application of PBE in the context of signaling games.

#### 3.2c Applications in Game Theory

The Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) has been widely applied in various fields of game theory. In this section, we will explore some of these applications, focusing on the signaling game and the market equilibrium computation game.

##### Signaling Game

The signaling game is a classic example of a game with incomplete information. In this game, a sender chooses a signal to send to a receiver, who then chooses an action based on the received signal. The sender's type (good or bad) is private information, and the receiver only observes the signal. The PBE has been used to analyze various versions of the signaling game, including the basic signaling game, the adverse selection game, and the moral hazard game.

In the basic signaling game, the sender chooses a signal to send to the receiver, who then chooses an action based on the received signal. The sender's type (good or bad) is private information, and the receiver only observes the signal. The PBE has been used to find the equilibrium strategies and beliefs in this game.

In the adverse selection game, the sender is a seller who chooses a price to sell a good to the receiver, who is a buyer. The seller's type is the quality of the good, which is private information. The PBE has been used to find the equilibrium strategies and beliefs in this game.

In the moral hazard game, the sender is an insurer who chooses a premium to insure the receiver, who is an insured. The insurer's type is the riskiness of the insured, which is private information. The PBE has been used to find the equilibrium strategies and beliefs in this game.

##### Market Equilibrium Computation Game

The market equilibrium computation game is another example of a game with incomplete information. In this game, players are buyers and sellers who interact in a market. Each player has private information about their type (e.g., their valuation for a good or their cost of production). The PBE has been used to compute the market equilibrium in this game.

The PBE has been used to find the equilibrium strategies and beliefs in this game. The PBE has also been used to analyze the robustness of the market equilibrium to small perturbations in the game. This is important in real-world applications, where markets are often subject to small changes over time.

In conclusion, the Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium is a powerful tool in game theory. It has been widely applied in various fields, including the signaling game and the market equilibrium computation game. Its properties, such as uniqueness, robustness, Bayesian consistency, Bayesian coherence, and subgame perfection, make it a valuable concept in the study of advanced game theory and mechanism design.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of incomplete information in microeconomic theory. We have explored the fundamental concepts and principles that govern the behavior of economic agents when they are operating under conditions of uncertainty. We have also examined the role of information in shaping economic outcomes, and how it can be used to design efficient mechanisms.

We have learned that incomplete information can lead to a variety of interesting phenomena, such as adverse selection, moral hazard, and the revelation of information. We have also seen how these phenomena can be addressed using various mechanisms, such as screening, signaling, and incentive compatibility.

Moreover, we have discussed the importance of Bayesian equilibrium in analyzing games with incomplete information. We have seen how it can be used to predict the behavior of rational agents in situations where they have private information about their own type.

In conclusion, understanding incomplete information is crucial for anyone studying microeconomic theory. It provides a powerful framework for analyzing a wide range of economic phenomena, and offers valuable insights into the design of efficient mechanisms.

#### 3.2b Examples of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

In the previous section, we defined the Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) and discussed its properties. In this section, we will explore some examples of PBE to gain a deeper understanding of this concept.

##### Example 1: Gift Game 1

Consider the following game:

For any value of $p$, Equilibrium 1 exists, a pooling equilibrium in which both types of sender choose the same action:

The sender prefers the payoff of 0 from not giving to the payoff of -1 from sending and not being accepted. Thus, "Give" has zero probability in equilibrium and Bayes's Rule does not restrict the belief "Prob(Friend|Give)" at all. That belief must be pessimistic enough that the receiver prefers the payoff of 0 from rejecting a gift to the expected payoff of $x (1) + (1-x)(-1) = 2x -1$, from accepting, so the requirement that the receiver's strategy maximize his expected payoff given his beliefs necessitates that "Prob(Friend|Give)"<math> \leq .5.</math> On the other hand, "Prob(Friend|Not give) = p" is required by Bayes's Rule, since both types take that action and it is uninformative about the sender's type.

If $p\geq 1/2$, a second pooling equilibrium exists as well as Equilibrium 1, based on different beliefs:

The sender prefers the payoff of 1 from giving to the payoff of 0 from not giving, expecting that his gift will be accepted. In equilibrium, Bayes's Rule requires the receiver to have the belief "Prob(Friend|Give) = p", since both types take that action and it is uninformative about the sender's type in this equilibrium. The out-of-equilibrium belief does not matter, since the sender would not want to deviate to "Not give" no matter what response the receiver would have.

Equilibrium 1 is perverse if $p \geq .5$. The game could have $p=.99$, so the sender is very likely a friend, but the receiver still would refuse any gift because he thinks enemies are much more likely than friends to give gifts. This shows how pessimistic beliefs can result in an equilibrium bad for both players, one that is not Pareto efficient. These beliefs seem unrealistic, though, and are often not observed in real-world scenarios.

##### Example 2: Gift Game 2

Consider the following game:

For any value of $p$, Equilibrium 2 exists, a separating equilibrium in which the two types of sender choose different actions:

The sender prefers the payoff of 1 from giving to the payoff of 0 from not giving, expecting that his gift will be accepted. In equilibrium, Bayes's Rule requires the receiver to have the belief "Prob(Friend|Give) = p", since both types take that action and it is uninformative about the sender's type in this equilibrium. The out-of-equilibrium belief does not matter, since the sender would not want to deviate to "Not give" no matter what response the receiver would have.

Equilibrium 2 is more realistic than Equilibrium 1, as it does not require the receiver to be overly pessimistic about the sender's type. However, it still assumes that the sender is always willing to give a gift, which may not be the case in all scenarios.

These examples illustrate the complexity of PBE and the importance of understanding the underlying beliefs and strategies of the players involved. They also highlight the need for further research to better understand the conditions under which PBE can be achieved and its implications for real-world scenarios.




#### 3.2c Application in Game Theory

In the previous sections, we have discussed the concept of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) and explored some examples of PBE. Now, we will delve into the application of PBE in game theory, specifically in the context of the game "Ô ăn quan".

##### 3.2c.1 Ô ăn quan and PBE

The game "Ô ăn quan" is a two-player game where each player has two possible strategies: "Ô" and "Quan". The game is played on a 3x3x3 board, and the first player to capture all three of their pieces wins the game. 

In the context of PBE, the game can be analyzed as follows:

1. The first player (Player 1) has two types: "Friend" and "Enemy". The type "Friend" has a payoff of 1 if they win and 0 if they lose, while the type "Enemy" has a payoff of -1 if they win and 0 if they lose.

2. The second player (Player 2) also has two types: "Friend" and "Enemy". The type "Friend" has a payoff of 1 if they win and 0 if they lose, while the type "Enemy" has a payoff of -1 if they win and 0 if they lose.

3. The strategy of Player 1 is to choose between "Ô" and "Quan". The strategy of Player 2 is to choose between "Ô" and "Quan".

4. The payoff matrix for Player 1 is as follows:

| Player 1's Strategy | Player 2's Strategy | Player 1's Payoff |
|--------------------|--------------------|-------------------|
| "Ô"               | "Ô"              | 1               |
| "Ô"               | "Quan"          | 0               |
| "Quan"           | "Ô"              | -1              |
| "Quan"           | "Quan"          | 0               |

5. The payoff matrix for Player 2 is the same as Player 1's, but with the roles reversed.

6. The PBE for this game is a pooling equilibrium, where both types of players choose the same strategy: "Ô". This equilibrium is perverse if the probability of a "Friend" is greater than 0.5, as discussed in the previous section.

In conclusion, the game "Ô ăn quan" provides a useful example for understanding the concept of PBE in game theory. By analyzing the game in the context of PBE, we can gain a deeper understanding of the strategies and payoffs involved in the game.




#### 3.3a Definition of Signaling Games

In the realm of game theory, signaling games are a type of dynamic Bayesian game that are characterized by one player taking an action, known as a signal, to convey information to another player. This action is more costly if the player is conveying false information. The essence of a signaling game lies in the tension between the sender and the receiver, where the sender wants to persuade the receiver that they have a desirable type, and the receiver interprets the signal to determine the sender's type.

A simple signaling game would have two players, the sender and the receiver. The sender has one of two types, "desirable" and "undesirable", with different payoff functions. The receiver, on the other hand, has just one type. The sender moves first, choosing an action called the "signal" or "message". The receiver moves second, after observing the signal. The payoffs for both players are dependent on the sender's type, the message chosen by the sender, and the action chosen by the receiver.

The concept of signaling games is closely tied to the concept of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE). In the context of signaling games, PBE is the equilibrium concept that is relevant. It is a refinement of Bayesian Nash equilibrium, and it is the equilibrium that is achieved in signaling games.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the application of signaling games in game theory, specifically in the context of the game "Ô ăn quan".

#### 3.3b Analysis of Signaling Games

In the analysis of signaling games, we often look for the Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE). This equilibrium concept is a refinement of Bayesian Nash equilibrium and is particularly relevant in signaling games. It is the equilibrium that is achieved in signaling games, where nature chooses the sender to have type $t$ with probability $p$. The sender then chooses the probability with which to take signaling action $m$, and the receiver chooses the probability with which to take action $a$.

The payoff for the sender is given by $u_s(t, m, a)$, and the payoff for the receiver is given by $u_r(t, m, a)$. The sender's payoff is a function of their type, the message they send, and the action taken by the receiver. Similarly, the receiver's payoff is a function of the sender's type, the message they send, and the action taken by the receiver.

The PBE is a set of strategies and beliefs that are self-enforcing. This means that each player's strategy is the best response to their beliefs about the other player's strategy, and each player's beliefs are rational given their own strategy and the strategy of the other player. In other words, the PBE is a set of strategies and beliefs that are mutually consistent and stable.

In the context of signaling games, the PBE can be used to analyze the strategies and beliefs of the players. For example, in the game "Ô ăn quan", the PBE can be used to determine the strategies and beliefs of the players, and to predict the outcome of the game.

In the next section, we will explore some examples of signaling games and how the PBE can be used to analyze these games.

#### 3.3c Applications in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore some applications of signaling games in game theory. We will focus on the game "Ô ăn quan" and how the Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) can be used to analyze the strategies and beliefs of the players.

##### Ô ăn quan and PBE

The game "Ô ăn quan" is a two-player game where each player has two possible strategies: "Ô" and "Quan". The game is played on a 3x3x3 board, and the first player to capture all three of their pieces wins the game.

In the context of signaling games, the game "Ô ăn quan" can be analyzed using the PBE. The sender (player 1) has two types: "Friend" and "Enemy". The type "Friend" has a payoff of 1 if they win and 0 if they lose, while the type "Enemy" has a payoff of -1 if they win and 0 if they lose.

The receiver (player 2) also has two types: "Friend" and "Enemy". The type "Friend" has a payoff of 1 if they win and 0 if they lose, while the type "Enemy" has a payoff of -1 if they win and 0 if they lose.

The strategy of player 1 is to choose between "Ô" and "Quan". The strategy of player 2 is to choose between "Ô" and "Quan".

The payoff matrix for player 1 is as follows:

| Player 1's Strategy | Player 2's Strategy | Player 1's Payoff |
|--------------------|--------------------|-------------------|
| "Ô"               | "Ô"              | 1               |
| "Ô"               | "Quan"          | 0               |
| "Quan"           | "Ô"              | -1              |
| "Quan"           | "Quan"          | 0               |

The payoff matrix for player 2 is the same as player 1, but with the roles reversed.

The PBE for this game is a pooling equilibrium, where both types of players choose the same strategy: "Ô". This equilibrium is perverse if the probability of a "Friend" is greater than 0.5, as discussed in the previous section.

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 4: Mechanism Design, a crucial component of our comprehensive guide to advanced game theory and microeconomic theory. This chapter will delve into the fascinating world of mechanism design, a field that combines elements of game theory, economics, and mathematics to design systems that achieve desired outcomes in the presence of strategic behavior.

Mechanism design is a powerful tool that has found applications in a wide range of fields, from auction design to voting systems. It provides a framework for understanding how decisions are made in situations where the decision-maker does not have complete information about the preferences of the participants. This is often the case in real-world scenarios, where individuals or groups may have private information about their preferences or abilities that they do not reveal to others.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of mechanism design, including the revelation principle, the implementation problem, and the concept of incentive compatibility. We will also discuss various solution concepts, such as the Vickrey-Clarke-Groves mechanism and the Groves mechanism, and their applications in different contexts.

We will also delve into the role of mechanism design in microeconomic theory. Microeconomic theory is concerned with the behavior of individuals and firms in markets, and mechanism design provides a powerful tool for understanding how these agents interact in situations where their preferences or abilities are not fully observable.

This chapter will provide a comprehensive overview of mechanism design, starting from the basics and gradually moving on to more advanced topics. We will use a combination of mathematical notation and real-world examples to illustrate the concepts and principles discussed.
