#### 3.3b Examples of Signaling Games

In this section, we will explore some examples of signaling games to further illustrate the concepts discussed in the previous sections. These examples will help us understand the dynamics of signaling games and how they can be applied in various scenarios.

##### Example 1: The Sir Philip Sidney Game

The Sir Philip Sidney game is a classic example of a signaling game. In this game, there are two individuals, the "signaler" and the "responder". The responder has some good which can be transferred to the signaler or not. The signaler can be in one of two states, "healthy" or "needy". If the signaler receives the good, his fitness will be 1. Otherwise his fitness will be (1-"b") or (1-"a") if healthy or needy respectively (where "a">"b"). The signaler can send a signal or not. If he sends the signal he incurs a cost of "c" regardless of the outcome.

The responder only wants to transfer the good to the needy signaler, but the signaler would want the good regardless of his state. This creates a partial conflict of interest, where there would be an incentive for deception. However, Maynard Smith showed that for certain values of "c", honest signaling can be an evolutionarily stable strategy. This suggests that it might be sustained by evolution.

##### Example 2: The Signaling Game with Asymmetric Information

In this example, we consider a signaling game with asymmetric information. There are two types of players, the "sender" and the "receiver". The sender has two types, "good" and "bad", with payoff functions $u_g(x)$ and $u_b(x)$ respectively. The receiver, on the other hand, has just one type. The sender moves first, choosing an action called the "signal". The receiver moves second, after observing the signal. The payoffs for both players are dependent on the sender's type, the message chosen by the sender, and the action chosen by the receiver.

The receiver's payoff function is given by $u_r(x)$, where $x$ is the receiver's action. The sender's payoff function is given by $u_s(x)$, where $x$ is the sender's action. The receiver's action is chosen based on the signal received from the sender. The sender's action is chosen based on the receiver's action.

This example illustrates the dynamics of a signaling game with asymmetric information. The sender has more information about his type than the receiver, and the receiver must make a decision based on the signal received from the sender. This game can be extended to include multiple types of senders and receivers, and different types of signals and actions.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the analysis of signaling games, focusing on the concept of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) and how it applies to these games.

#### Example 3: The Signaling Game with Symmetric Information

In this example, we consider a signaling game with symmetric information. This game is similar to the previous example, but with a key difference: both players have the same information about the game. This means that both players know the payoff functions of the other player, and both players know the probability with which the other player will take a particular action.

The game is played between two players, the "sender" and the "receiver". The sender has two types, "good" and "bad", with payoff functions $u_g(x)$ and $u_b(x)$ respectively. The receiver, also has two types, "good" and "bad", with payoff functions $u_r(x)$ and $u_b(x)$ respectively. The sender moves first, choosing an action called the "signal". The receiver moves second, after observing the signal. The payoffs for both players are dependent on the sender's type, the message chosen by the sender, and the action chosen by the receiver.

The receiver's payoff function is given by $u_r(x)$, where $x$ is the receiver's action. The sender's payoff function is given by $u_s(x)$, where $x$ is the sender's action. The receiver's action is chosen based on the signal received from the sender. The sender's action is chosen based on the receiver's action.

This example illustrates the dynamics of a signaling game with symmetric information. Both players have the same information about the game, and both players can make strategic decisions based on this information. This game can be extended to include multiple types of senders and receivers, and different types of signals and actions.

We have explored the fundamental concepts and principles that govern the behavior of economic agents in situations where they do not have complete information about the market or the other agents in the market. We have also examined the implications of incomplete information for market outcomes and the strategies that agents can use to navigate these complexities.

We have seen that incomplete information can lead to a variety of interesting and often counterintuitive outcomes. For example, we have learned that in a market with asymmetric information, the quality of a good can be affected by the actions of the seller, even if the seller has no direct influence over the quality of the good itself. We have also seen that in a market with incomplete information, agents can use signaling and screening mechanisms to extract information about the quality of a good or the type of an agent.

Incomplete information is a fundamental aspect of many economic situations, and understanding it is crucial for making sense of the behavior of economic agents and the outcomes of economic markets. By studying incomplete information, we can gain insights into the workings of the economy and develop strategies for navigating these complexities.

Next, we will explore the various types of mechanisms, including direct mechanisms, indirect mechanisms, and hybrid mechanisms. We will also discuss the conditions under which these mechanisms can be implemented efficiently. 

Finally, we will examine some of the challenges and limitations of mechanism design, such as the revelation principle and the impossibility theorem. We will also discuss some of the recent advancements in mechanism design, such as the use of auctions and lotteries.

Throughout this chapter, we will use mathematical notation to express the key concepts and principles of mechanism design. For example, we might denote the set of all possible strategies for a player as `$S$`, and the payoff function for a player as `$u(s)$`, where `$s \in S$`.

#### 3.3c Application in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore the applications of signaling games in game theory. Signaling games are a fundamental concept in game theory, and they have been applied in various fields, including economics, biology, and political science.

##### Signaling Games in Economics

In economics, signaling games are used to model situations where one party has more information than the other. For example, in the labor market, employers often have less information about potential employees than the employees themselves. This information asymmetry can lead to signaling games, where employees can signal their ability or effort level to potential employers.

One of the most famous examples of a signaling game in economics is the "lemon market" model proposed by George Akerlof. In this model, Akerlof considers a market for used cars, where the quality of the car is not observable to the buyer. The seller, who knows the quality of the car, can signal the quality by offering a warranty. The buyer, who does not know the quality, can then infer the quality from the length of the warranty. This model illustrates how signaling games can be used to solve problems of asymmetric information in markets.

##### Signaling Games in Biology

In biology, signaling games are used to model interactions between different species. For example, in the Sir Philip Sidney game, the signaler and the responder can be interpreted as two different species. The responder wants to transfer a good to the needy signaler, but the signaler would want the good regardless of his state. This creates a partial conflict of interest, where there would be an incentive for deception. However, Maynard Smith showed that for certain values of "c", honest signaling can be an evolutionarily stable strategy. This suggests that it might be sustained by evolution.

##### Signaling Games in Political Science

In political science, signaling games are used to model situations where one party has more information than the other. For example, in voting behavior, voters often have more information about their preferences than the political parties. This information asymmetry can lead to signaling games, where voters can signal their preferences to political parties.

One of the most famous examples of a signaling game in political science is the "voter-candidate signaling game" proposed by Anthony Downs. In this game, Downs considers a political market where voters have private information about their preferences and candidates have private information about their policies. The voters can signal their preferences to the candidates by voting, and the candidates can signal their policies by campaigning. This model illustrates how signaling games can be used to understand voting behavior and political campaigns.

In conclusion, signaling games are a powerful tool in game theory, with applications in various fields. They provide a framework for understanding situations where one party has more information than the other, and they can be used to model a wide range of phenomena, from labor markets to political campaigns.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of incomplete information in microeconomic theory. We have explored the fundamental concepts and principles that govern this area, and how they apply to various economic scenarios. We have also examined the role of information in economic decision-making, and how it can influence outcomes.

We have learned that incomplete information can lead to strategic behavior, as agents may have incentives to misrepresent their true information. This can result in inefficiencies and suboptimal outcomes. However, we have also seen how mechanisms such as signaling and screening can help to mitigate these problems.

Furthermore, we have discussed the importance of understanding the informational foundations of economic models. By considering the assumptions and implications of incomplete information, we can gain a deeper understanding of economic phenomena and design more effective policies and interventions.

In conclusion, incomplete information is a crucial aspect of microeconomic theory. It is a complex and multifaceted topic, but by understanding its fundamental principles and mechanisms, we can gain valuable insights into economic behavior and decision-making.

## Chapter: Chapter 4: Mechanism Design

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 4 of "Microeconomic Theory II: A Comprehensive Guide to Advanced Game Theory and Mechanism Design". This chapter is dedicated to the fascinating field of mechanism design, a critical component of game theory. 

Mechanism design is a mathematical framework used to design rules or mechanisms that can be used to achieve a desired outcome in a strategic environment. It is often used in situations where there are multiple decision-makers, each with their own preferences and information. The goal of mechanism design is to design a mechanism that will induce the decision-makers to report their true preferences, leading to an optimal outcome.

In this chapter, we will delve into the intricacies of mechanism design, exploring its principles, applications, and challenges. We will start by introducing the basic concepts of mechanism design, including the key players, their preferences, and the mechanism itself. We will then move on to discuss the different types of mechanisms, such as auctions, voting systems, and matching mechanisms, and how they can be designed to achieve desired outcomes.

We will also explore the concept of incentive compatibility, a fundamental principle in mechanism design that ensures that the decision-makers have an incentive to report their true preferences. We will discuss how incentive compatibility can be achieved, and the implications of violating it.

Finally, we will touch upon the challenges and limitations of mechanism design, such as the revelation principle and the curse of dimensionality. We will also discuss some of the recent advancements in mechanism design, such as the use of algorithmic mechanisms and the incorporation of behavioral economics insights.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of mechanism design, its principles, applications, and challenges. You will be equipped with the knowledge and tools to design and analyze mechanisms in a variety of strategic environments. So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of mechanism design.




### Subsection: 3.4a Definition of Screening Games

Screening games are a type of principal-agent game where one player, known as the principal, offers a contract to another player, known as the agent. The agent then chooses whether to accept the contract or not, based on their private information about their ability or type. This game is used to model situations where one party has more information about their own abilities or characteristics than the other party.

In a screening game, the principal offers a contract to the agent, which includes a wage level and possibly other terms. The agent then chooses the amount of credentials or effort they will exert, and whether to accept the contract or not. The principal, who does not know the agent's type, must decide whether to offer a contract that is attractive enough for the agent to accept, but not so attractive that it is not worth the principal's while.

The key feature of screening games is that the agent's decision to accept or reject a contract is based on their private information about their type. This information is not directly observable to the principal, and the agent can use this to their advantage. For example, an agent with a high ability may choose to accept a contract with a lower wage level, knowing that the principal will not be able to offer a higher wage to an agent with a lower ability.

Screening games are closely related to signaling games, but there is a difference in how information is exchanged. In signaling games, the agent chooses the amount of effort or credentials they will exert before the contract is offered. In screening games, the agent chooses the amount of credentials or effort after the contract is offered. This difference can lead to different outcomes and strategies in the two types of games.

Some economists use the terms signaling and screening interchangeably, but the distinction can be attributed to Stiglitz and Weiss (1989). They argue that signaling games are used to model situations where the agent's effort or ability is observable to the principal, while screening games are used to model situations where the agent's type is not directly observable.

In the next section, we will explore the applications of screening games in various fields, including economics, biology, and political science.

### Subsection: 3.4b Analysis of Screening Games

In the analysis of screening games, we focus on the strategies and outcomes that can arise from the interactions between the principal and the agent. The principal's goal is to offer a contract that attracts the agent with the highest ability, while the agent's goal is to maximize their payoff by choosing the optimal amount of effort or credentials.

#### 3.4b.1 Strategies and Payoffs

The principal can offer a contract with a higher wage level and better terms, but this comes at a cost. The principal must balance the desire to attract the best agent with the need to limit their own costs. The agent, on the other hand, can choose to exert more effort or acquire more credentials, which can increase their payoff but also comes at a cost in terms of time and resources.

The payoff for the principal is typically defined as the difference between the value of the agent's output and the cost of the contract. The payoff for the agent is the difference between the value of the contract and the cost of exerting effort or acquiring credentials.

#### 3.4b.2 Equilibrium and Efficiency

In a screening game, the equilibrium outcome is the contract that maximizes the payoff for both the principal and the agent. This contract is typically efficient, meaning that it maximizes the total payoff for both parties. However, the efficiency of the equilibrium outcome depends on the specific characteristics of the game, including the number of agents, the distribution of abilities, and the cost of exerting effort or acquiring credentials.

#### 3.4b.3 Applications of Screening Games

Screening games have been applied to a wide range of real-world situations, including job markets, dating markets, and markets for education. In these applications, the principal can be a firm, a potential spouse, or an educational institution, and the agent can be a job seeker, a potential partner, or a student.

In the context of job markets, screening games can be used to model the hiring process. The principal, a firm, offers a contract to the agent, a job seeker. The job seeker chooses the amount of effort they will exert or the credentials they will acquire, and whether to accept the contract or not. The firm, who does not know the job seeker's ability, must decide whether to offer a contract that is attractive enough for the job seeker to accept, but not so attractive that it is not worth the firm's while.

In the context of dating markets, screening games can be used to model the process of finding a partner. The principal, a potential spouse, offers a contract to the agent, a potential partner. The potential partner chooses the amount of effort they will exert or the credentials they will acquire, and whether to accept the contract or not. The potential spouse, who does not know the potential partner's characteristics, must decide whether to offer a contract that is attractive enough for the potential partner to accept, but not so attractive that it is not worth the potential spouse's while.

In the context of markets for education, screening games can be used to model the process of choosing a school or program. The principal, an educational institution, offers a contract to the agent, a student. The student chooses the amount of effort they will exert or the credentials they will acquire, and whether to accept the contract or not. The educational institution, who does not know the student's ability, must decide whether to offer a contract that is attractive enough for the student to accept, but not so attractive that it is not worth the educational institution's while.

In the next section, we will explore the role of screening games in mechanism design, where the principal is a mechanism designer and the agent is a participant in a market.

### Subsection: 3.4c Case Studies in Screening Games

In this section, we will delve into some case studies that illustrate the application of screening games in real-world scenarios. These case studies will provide a deeper understanding of the principles and strategies involved in screening games.

#### 3.4c.1 Case Study 1: Job Market Screening

Consider a job market where a firm is hiring a worker. The worker has a certain level of ability, but this ability is not directly observable to the firm. The firm offers a wage level and other terms of employment, and the worker chooses the amount of effort they will exert and whether to accept the contract or not.

The firm's goal is to offer a contract that attracts the worker with the highest ability, while the worker's goal is to maximize their payoff by choosing the optimal amount of effort. The equilibrium outcome is the contract that maximizes the payoff for both the firm and the worker, which is typically efficient.

However, the efficiency of the equilibrium outcome depends on the specific characteristics of the game, including the distribution of abilities among workers, the cost of exerting effort, and the cost of the contract for the firm. For example, if the distribution of abilities is skewed, with many workers having low ability, the equilibrium outcome may not be efficient.

#### 3.4c.2 Case Study 2: Dating Market Screening

In a dating market, a potential partner offers a contract to a potential spouse. The potential spouse chooses the amount of effort they will exert or the credentials they will acquire, and whether to accept the contract or not.

The potential partner's goal is to offer a contract that attracts the potential spouse with the highest desirability, while the potential spouse's goal is to maximize their payoff by choosing the optimal amount of effort. The equilibrium outcome is the contract that maximizes the payoff for both the potential partner and the potential spouse, which is typically efficient.

However, the efficiency of the equilibrium outcome depends on the specific characteristics of the game, including the distribution of desirability among potential spouses, the cost of exerting effort, and the cost of the contract for the potential partner. For example, if the distribution of desirability is skewed, with many potential spouses having low desirability, the equilibrium outcome may not be efficient.

These case studies illustrate the principles and strategies involved in screening games. They show how the principal and agent interact to reach an equilibrium outcome that is typically efficient, but which may not be efficient if the distribution of abilities or desirability is skewed.

We have explored the fundamental concepts and principles that govern this area, and have seen how they can be applied to a variety of real-world scenarios. We have also examined the role of screening games and mechanism design in managing incomplete information, and have seen how these tools can be used to optimize outcomes in a variety of economic contexts.

We have also seen how incomplete information can lead to market failures, and how these failures can be addressed through various mechanisms. We have explored the role of signaling games in managing incomplete information, and have seen how these games can be used to optimize outcomes in a variety of economic contexts.

In conclusion, understanding incomplete information is crucial for anyone seeking to understand the complex dynamics of microeconomic theory. It is a field that is constantly evolving, and one that offers a wealth of opportunities for further exploration and research.

## Chapter: Chapter 4: Asymmetric Information

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 4 of "Microeconomic Theory II: A Comprehensive Guide to Advanced Game Theory and Mechanism Design". This chapter delves into the fascinating world of asymmetric information, a fundamental concept in microeconomic theory. Asymmetric information refers to situations where one party has more information than the other, leading to information asymmetry. This information asymmetry can significantly impact the outcomes of economic transactions, making it a crucial topic for understanding the functioning of markets and the behavior of economic agents.

In this chapter, we will explore the theory of asymmetric information in depth. We will start by defining what asymmetric information is and how it arises in economic transactions. We will then delve into the various models of asymmetric information, including the adverse selection model, the moral hazard model, and the signaling model. These models will help us understand how information asymmetry can lead to market failures and how it can be addressed.

We will also discuss the role of mechanism design in managing asymmetric information. Mechanism design is a powerful tool that can be used to design rules and incentives to optimize outcomes in situations where there is asymmetric information. We will explore some of the key mechanisms used in the literature, such as the Vickrey auction and the screening mechanism.

Finally, we will look at some real-world applications of asymmetric information. These applications will help us understand how the theory of asymmetric information can be used to analyze and solve real-world economic problems.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to asymmetric information, equipping you with the knowledge and tools to understand and analyze situations where there is asymmetric information. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practitioner, we hope that this chapter will enhance your understanding of microeconomic theory and its applications.




### Subsection: 3.4b Examples of Screening Games

Screening games are a fundamental concept in game theory and have been applied to various real-world scenarios. In this section, we will explore some examples of screening games and how they can be used to model and analyze different situations.

#### 3.4b.1 Job Market Screening Game

One of the most common examples of screening games is the job market. In this game, the principal is the employer, and the agent is the job applicant. The employer offers a contract to the job applicant, which includes a salary and benefits. The job applicant then chooses the amount of effort they will exert in the job and whether to accept the contract or not. The employer, who does not know the job applicant's ability, must decide whether to offer a contract that is attractive enough for the job applicant to accept, but not so attractive that it is not worth the employer's while.

This game can be used to model the job market, where employers and job applicants interact to determine the terms of employment. The job applicant's decision to accept or reject a contract is based on their private information about their ability, which can include their education, experience, and skills. The employer, on the other hand, must make a decision based on the information they have about the job applicant, which may include their resume, references, and interview performance.

#### 3.4b.2 Contract Bridge Screening Game

Another example of a screening game is the game of contract bridge. In this game, the principal is the declarer, and the agents are the defenders. The declarer offers a contract to the defenders, which includes the number of tricks they must win to make the contract. The defenders then choose the number of tricks they will try to take and whether to accept the contract or not. The declarer, who does not know the defenders' cards, must decide whether to offer a contract that is attractive enough for the defenders to accept, but not so attractive that it is not worth the declarer's while.

This game can be used to model situations where one party has more information about their abilities or characteristics than the other party. In contract bridge, the declarer has more information about their cards than the defenders, and the defenders must make decisions based on their private information about their cards. This game can also be extended to other situations, such as in negotiations or auctions, where one party has more information than the other.

#### 3.4b.3 Online Dating Screening Game

Online dating has become a popular example of a screening game. In this game, the principal is the dater, and the agent is the potential date. The dater creates a profile and sends a message to the potential date, who then decides whether to respond or not. The dater, who does not know the potential date's appearance or personality, must decide whether to send a message that is attractive enough for the potential date to respond, but not so attractive that it is not worth the dater's while.

This game can be used to model the dating market, where daters and potential dates interact to determine the terms of a potential relationship. The potential date's decision to respond or not is based on their private information about their appearance and personality, which can include their physical attractiveness, interests, and values. The dater, on the other hand, must make a decision based on the information they have about the potential date, which may include their profile picture, bio, and communication style.

### Conclusion

Screening games are a powerful tool for analyzing situations where one party has more information about their abilities or characteristics than the other party. By understanding the key features of screening games and exploring different examples, we can gain a deeper understanding of how these games can be used to model and analyze various real-world scenarios. 





### Subsection: 3.4c Application in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore the application of screening games in game theory. Screening games are a fundamental concept in game theory and have been applied to various real-world scenarios. In this section, we will focus on the application of screening games in game theory, specifically in the context of mechanism design.

#### 3.4c.1 Mechanism Design and Screening Games

Mechanism design is a branch of game theory that deals with the design of mechanisms or rules to achieve a desired outcome in a strategic environment. Screening games are often used in mechanism design to model situations where one party has private information about their preferences or characteristics, and the other party must make a decision based on this information.

One example of this is the job market, where the employer must make a decision about the terms of employment based on the job applicant's private information about their ability. This can be modeled as a screening game, where the employer is the principal and the job applicant is the agent. The employer offers a contract to the job applicant, who then chooses the amount of effort they will exert in the job and whether to accept the contract or not. The employer, who does not know the job applicant's ability, must decide whether to offer a contract that is attractive enough for the job applicant to accept, but not so attractive that it is not worth the employer's while.

#### 3.4c.2 Screening Games in Mechanism Design

In mechanism design, screening games are often used to design mechanisms that achieve a desired outcome, such as maximizing social welfare or minimizing costs. These mechanisms must take into account the strategic behavior of the agents, who may have private information about their preferences or characteristics.

One example of this is the design of a mechanism to allocate resources among a set of agents. In this scenario, the principal is the resource allocator, and the agents are the resource seekers. The resource allocator must decide how to allocate the resources among the agents, who may have private information about their valuation of the resources. This can be modeled as a screening game, where the resource allocator is the principal and the resource seekers are the agents. The resource allocator offers a resource allocation mechanism to the resource seekers, who then choose whether to accept or reject the allocation. The resource allocator, who does not know the resource seekers' valuation of the resources, must decide whether to offer an allocation that is attractive enough for the resource seekers to accept, but not so attractive that it is not worth the resource allocator's while.

#### 3.4c.3 Conclusion

In conclusion, screening games have a wide range of applications in game theory, particularly in mechanism design. By modeling situations where one party has private information about their preferences or characteristics, screening games allow us to design mechanisms that achieve a desired outcome in a strategic environment. As such, they are a fundamental concept in game theory and have important implications for various real-world scenarios.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of understanding the information available to each player in a game. This understanding can help players make more informed decisions and potentially improve their outcomes.

Another important concept we have explored is the role of mechanism design in dealing with incomplete information. Mechanism design provides a framework for designing games that can elicit truthful information from players and achieve desired outcomes. Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive guide to understanding incomplete information in game theory and mechanism design. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the topic of mechanism design, specifically focusing on the revelation principle.

The revelation principle is a fundamental concept in mechanism design that helps us understand the relationship between a mechanism and its underlying game. It states that any mechanism that is incentive compatible can be simplified to a direct mechanism. This means that we can design a mechanism that achieves the desired outcome without having to worry about strategic behavior from players.

In this chapter, we will explore the revelation principle in detail. We will start by discussing the concept of incentive compatibility and how it relates to the revelation principle. We will then move on to explore different types of mechanisms and how they can be simplified using the revelation principle. Finally, we will discuss some applications of the revelation principle in real-world scenarios.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of the revelation principle and its applications in mechanism design. This knowledge will not only help you in understanding more complex games and mechanisms, but also in designing mechanisms that achieve desired outcomes in a strategic environment. So let's dive in and explore the world of mechanism design through the lens of the revelation principle.

## Chapter: Microeconomic Theory II: A Comprehensive Guide to Advanced Game Theory and Mechanism Design

In the previous chapter, we explored the fundamentals of microeconomic theory, including consumer and producer behavior, market equilibrium, and welfare economics. ## Chapter 4: Market Design:




### Introduction

In the previous chapters, we have explored the fundamentals of microeconomic theory, game theory, and mechanism design. We have also delved into static games, where players make decisions simultaneously or sequentially without the ability to observe the actions of other players. However, in many real-world scenarios, decisions are not made in isolation, but rather in a dynamic environment where players interact with each other over time. This is where dynamic games with incomplete information come into play.

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of dynamic games with incomplete information. We will explore how players make decisions in a dynamic setting, where they can observe the actions of other players and adapt their strategies accordingly. We will also examine how incomplete information can affect the outcome of these games, and how players can use this information to their advantage.

We will begin by discussing the concept of dynamic games, and how they differ from static games. We will then move on to explore the different types of incomplete information that can exist in these games, such as private information, public information, and common knowledge. We will also discuss the role of signaling in these games, and how it can be used to convey information about a player's type.

Next, we will delve into the concept of mechanism design, and how it can be used to design games with desirable outcomes. We will explore the different types of mechanisms that can be used, such as auctions, voting systems, and matching markets. We will also discuss the challenges of mechanism design, such as the revelation principle and the implementation problem.

Finally, we will examine some real-world applications of dynamic games with incomplete information, such as bargaining, contract theory, and the prisoner's dilemma. We will also discuss some of the key insights and lessons learned from these applications.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of dynamic games with incomplete information, and be equipped with the tools to analyze and design these games in a variety of real-world scenarios. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of dynamic games with incomplete information.




### Subsection: 4.1a Definition of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium

In the previous chapter, we introduced the concept of Nash equilibrium, a solution concept in game theory where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy. In this section, we will extend this concept to dynamic games with incomplete information, specifically focusing on Bayesian Nash equilibrium.

Bayesian Nash equilibrium (BNE) is a solution concept in game theory that extends the concept of Nash equilibrium to games with incomplete information. It is named after the British mathematician Thomas Bayes, who first introduced the concept of Bayesian probability. In a Bayesian Nash equilibrium, each player's strategy maximizes their expected payoff given their beliefs about the state of nature.

To understand Bayesian Nash equilibrium, let's consider a Bayesian game with two players, A and B, and two possible states of nature, s1 and s2. Player A has two types, type 1 and type 2, with probabilities p1 and p2 respectively. Player B has two types, type 1 and type 2, with probabilities q1 and q2 respectively. The payoff matrix for player A is given by:

| Player A's Payoff Matrix |
| --- | --- |
| Type | State |
| 1 | s1 | s2 |
| 2 | s1 | s2 |

The payoff matrix for player B is given by:

| Player B's Payoff Matrix |
| --- | --- |
| Type | State |
| 1 | s1 | s2 |
| 2 | s1 | s2 |

In this game, player A's type is private information, while player B's type is common knowledge. This means that player A's type is only known to player A, while player B's type is known to both players.

A Bayesian Nash equilibrium is a strategy profile that maximizes the expected payoff for each player given their beliefs and given the strategies played by the other players. That is, a strategy profile $\sigma$ is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium if and only if for every player $i$, keeping the strategies of every other player fixed, strategy $\sigma_i$ maximizes the expected payoff of player $i$ according to that player's beliefs.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of Bayesian Nash equilibrium in more detail, discussing its properties and how it can be used to analyze dynamic games with incomplete information.

### Subsection: 4.1b Properties of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium

Bayesian Nash equilibrium (BNE) has several important properties that make it a useful solution concept in game theory. These properties are derived from the fundamental principles of Bayesian Nash equilibrium and provide insights into the behavior of players in dynamic games with incomplete information.

#### 1. Existence

The existence of a Bayesian Nash equilibrium is a fundamental property that ensures the stability of the game. It states that for every Bayesian game with a finite number of players and states, there exists at least one Bayesian Nash equilibrium. This property is crucial as it guarantees that the game will eventually reach a stable state where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

#### 2. Uniqueness

The uniqueness of a Bayesian Nash equilibrium is a desirable property that ensures the predictability of the game. It states that for every Bayesian game with a finite number of players and states, there exists at most one Bayesian Nash equilibrium. This property is important as it simplifies the analysis of the game and allows us to make predictions about the behavior of players.

#### 3. Robustness

The robustness of a Bayesian Nash equilibrium is a property that ensures the stability of the game against small perturbations. It states that if a Bayesian Nash equilibrium exists for a given game, then it also exists for a slightly modified version of the game. This property is useful in real-world applications where the game may not be perfectly known or may be subject to small changes over time.

#### 4. Subgame Perfection

The subgame perfection of a Bayesian Nash equilibrium is a property that ensures the optimality of the equilibrium. It states that if a Bayesian Nash equilibrium exists for a given game, then it is also a subgame perfect equilibrium. This property is important as it ensures that the equilibrium is not only stable but also optimal for all players.

#### 5. Bayesian Rationality

The Bayesian rationality of a Bayesian Nash equilibrium is a property that ensures the rationality of players in the game. It states that if a Bayesian Nash equilibrium exists for a given game, then all players are Bayesian rational. This property is crucial as it ensures that players are making optimal decisions given their beliefs and the strategies of other players.

In the next section, we will explore how these properties can be used to analyze dynamic games with incomplete information.

### Subsection: 4.1c Applications of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium

Bayesian Nash equilibrium (BNE) has a wide range of applications in various fields, including economics, political science, and biology. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and how the properties of BNE are used to analyze these games.

#### 4.1c.1 Market Equilibrium

One of the most common applications of BNE is in the analysis of market equilibrium. In a market game, players are buyers and sellers who interact to determine the price and quantity of a good. The BNE in this game is the price and quantity that maximize the total surplus of all players.

The existence and uniqueness of a BNE in this game are crucial for the market to function efficiently. If there exists a BNE, then the market is stable and the price and quantity determined by the BNE are optimal for all players. This ensures that the market is efficient and that resources are allocated in the best possible way.

#### 4.1c.2 Political Voting

Another important application of BNE is in the analysis of political voting. In a political voting game, players are voters who vote for a candidate based on their beliefs and the beliefs of other voters. The BNE in this game is the voting strategy that maximizes the probability of the candidate with the highest utility being elected.

The robustness of a BNE is particularly useful in this game. Since the beliefs of voters are often uncertain, the robustness of a BNE ensures that the game will reach a stable state even if the beliefs of voters change slightly. This is important for the stability of the political system.

#### 4.1c.3 Evolutionary Games

BNE also has applications in evolutionary games, where players are organisms that interact and evolve over time. The BNE in these games is the strategy that maximizes the fitness of all players.

The subgame perfection of a BNE is particularly useful in these games. Since the strategies of players evolve over time, the subgame perfection of a BNE ensures that the equilibrium is not only stable but also optimal for all players at all stages of the game. This is crucial for the long-term stability of the system.

#### 4.1c.4 Mechanism Design

Finally, BNE has applications in mechanism design, where a designer creates a mechanism to elicit information from players and determine an optimal outcome. The BNE in this game is the mechanism that maximizes the expected payoff of the designer.

The Bayesian rationality of a BNE is particularly useful in this game. Since the designer is often uncertain about the beliefs of players, the Bayesian rationality of a BNE ensures that the designer can make optimal decisions given this uncertainty. This is crucial for the effectiveness of the mechanism.

In conclusion, Bayesian Nash equilibrium is a powerful tool for analyzing dynamic games with incomplete information. Its properties provide insights into the behavior of players and the stability of the game, making it a valuable concept in various fields.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of dynamic games with incomplete information. We have explored the fundamental concepts and principles that govern these games, and how they differ from static games. We have also examined the role of information in these games, and how it can be used to strategically advantage players.

We have learned that dynamic games with incomplete information are characterized by the fact that players do not have complete information about the game, and that this information can change over time. This adds a layer of complexity to the game, as players must not only make decisions based on their current information, but also anticipate how this information may change in the future.

We have also seen how these games can be modeled and analyzed using various techniques, such as Bayesian Nash equilibrium and subgame perfect equilibrium. These techniques allow us to predict the behavior of players in these games, and to understand the strategic implications of different decisions.

In conclusion, dynamic games with incomplete information are a rich and complex field of study. They provide a powerful framework for understanding strategic decision-making in a wide range of contexts, from economics and politics to biology and psychology. By mastering the concepts and techniques presented in this chapter, you will be well-equipped to tackle more advanced topics in game theory and mechanism design.

Consider a dynamic game with incomplete information. Suppose that player A has private information about their type, which can be either type 1 or type 2. Player B has no information about player A's type. If player A is of type 1, their payoff is 3 if they choose strategy 1 and 2 if they choose strategy 2. If player A is of type 2, their payoff is 4 if they choose strategy 1 and 1 if they choose strategy 2. Player B's payoff is 2 if player A chooses strategy 1 and 1 if player A chooses strategy 2. What is the Bayesian Nash equilibrium of this game?

If player A is of type 2, their payoff is 4 if they choose strategy 1 and 1 if they choose strategy 2. Player B's payoff is 2 if player A chooses strategy 1 and 1 if player A chooses strategy 2. What is the subgame perfect equilibrium of this game?

If player A is of type 2, their payoff is 4 if they choose strategy 1 and 1 if they choose strategy 2. Player B's payoff is 2 if player A chooses strategy 1 and 1 if player A chooses strategy 2. If player A is of type 2, their payoff is 4 if they choose strategy 1 and 1 if they choose strategy 2. Player B's payoff is 2 if player A chooses strategy 1 and 1 if player A chooses strategy 2. Player B has no information about player A's type. If player A is of type 1, their payoff is 3 if they choose strategy 1 and 2 if they choose strategy 2. If player A is of type 2, their payoff is 4 if they choose strategy 1 and 1 if they choose strategy 2. Player B's payoff is 2 if player A chooses strategy 1 and 1 if player A chooses strategy 2. ## Chapter: Chapter 5: Dynamic Games with Complete Information:

### Introduction

In the previous chapters, we have explored the fundamentals of game theory, mechanism design, and dynamic games with incomplete information. Now, we delve into the fascinating world of dynamic games with complete information. This chapter will provide a comprehensive understanding of these games, their characteristics, and the strategies involved.

Dynamic games with complete information are a class of games where all players have complete information about the game, including the strategies and payoffs of all other players. This is a significant departure from the games we have studied so far, where players often have incomplete information about the game or their opponents. This difference leads to unique challenges and opportunities in the design and analysis of these games.

We will begin by defining and discussing the key concepts and principles of dynamic games with complete information. We will then explore various types of these games, including simultaneous-move games, sequential-move games, and repeated games. We will also delve into the strategies and equilibria of these games, including the famous Nash equilibrium and its variants.

Throughout this chapter, we will use mathematical models and notation to describe and analyze these games. For example, we might represent a game as a matrix of payoffs, or use the notation $y_j(n)$ to denote the payoff of player j in game y at time n. We will also use the powerful tools of calculus and differential equations to analyze the dynamics of these games.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of dynamic games with complete information, and be equipped with the tools to analyze and design these games in a variety of contexts. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practitioner in the field of economics, political science, or biology, this chapter will provide you with valuable insights into the strategic interactions that occur in dynamic games with complete information.




### Subsection: 4.1b Examples of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium

In this section, we will explore some examples of Bayesian Nash equilibrium to further understand this concept.

#### Example 1: The Sheriff's Dilemma

Consider the game of the Sheriff's Dilemma, where a sheriff faces an armed suspect. Both must simultaneously decide whether to shoot the other or not. The suspect can either be of type "criminal" or type "civilian". The sheriff has only one type. The suspect knows its type and the Sheriff's type, but the Sheriff does not know the suspect's type. Thus, there is incomplete information (because the suspect has private information), making it a Bayesian game.

The payoff matrix for the Sheriff is given by:

| Sheriff's Payoff Matrix |
| --- | --- |
| Shoot | Do not shoot |
| Criminal | 10 | -10 |
| Civilian | -10 | 0 |

The payoff matrix for the Suspect is given by:

| Suspect's Payoff Matrix |
| --- | --- |
| Shoot | Do not shoot |
| Criminal | 10 | -10 |
| Civilian | -10 | 0 |

In this game, the Sheriff's strategy is to shoot if the suspect shoots, or not shoot if the suspect does not. The Suspect's strategy is to shoot if he is a criminal, even if the Sheriff does not shoot, but to not shoot if he is a civilian, even if the Sheriff shoots. This strategy profile is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium, as each player's strategy maximizes their expected payoff given their beliefs about the state of nature.

#### Example 2: The Market Equilibrium Computation

Consider a market with two firms competing on price. The market demand is given by $P = 100 - 2Q$, where $P$ is the price and $Q$ is the total quantity. Each firm has a cost of $C = 10Q$. The firms are identical and have perfect information about the market.

The payoff matrix for each firm is given by:

| Firm's Payoff Matrix |
| --- | --- |
| Price | Quantity |
| 10 | 20 |
| 15 | 15 |
| 20 | 10 |
| 25 | 5 |
| 30 | 0 |

In this game, the firms' strategies are to choose a price and quantity. The strategy profile where both firms choose a price of 10 and a quantity of 20 is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium, as each firm's strategy maximizes their expected payoff given their beliefs about the market.

These examples illustrate the concept of Bayesian Nash equilibrium in dynamic games with incomplete information. In the next section, we will explore the concept of subgame perfect equilibrium, which is a stronger solution concept than Bayesian Nash equilibrium.




### Subsection: 4.1c Application in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore some applications of Bayesian Nash equilibrium in game theory. These applications will help us understand the practical implications of the concepts we have learned.

#### Example 1: The Prisoner's Dilemma

The Prisoner's Dilemma is a classic game in game theory that illustrates the concept of Bayesian Nash equilibrium. In this game, two prisoners are interrogated separately and each has the option to confess or remain silent. If both remain silent, they each serve a short sentence. If both confess, they each serve a longer sentence. If one confesses and the other remains silent, the confessor goes free while the other serves a longer sentence.

The payoff matrix for each player is given by:

| Player's Payoff Matrix |
| --- | --- |
| Confess | Remain Silent |
| Confess | 10 | 10 |
| Remain Silent | 0 | 0 |

In this game, the strategy profile (Confess, Confess) is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium, as each player's strategy maximizes their expected payoff given their beliefs about the state of nature. However, this outcome is not Pareto efficient, as both players would be better off if they both remained silent.

#### Example 2: The Market Equilibrium Computation

The Market Equilibrium Computation is another application of Bayesian Nash equilibrium in game theory. In this game, two firms compete on price and quantity in a market. The firms have perfect information about the market and choose their strategies simultaneously.

The payoff matrix for each firm is given by:

| Firm's Payoff Matrix |
| --- | --- |
| Price | Quantity |
| 10 | 20 |
| 15 | 15 |
| 20 | 10 |
| 25 | 5 |
| 30 | 0 |

In this game, the strategy profile (Price = 10, Quantity = 20) is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium, as each firm's strategy maximizes their expected payoff given their beliefs about the state of nature. This outcome is also Pareto efficient, as no player can increase their payoff without decreasing another player's payoff.

These examples illustrate the power and versatility of Bayesian Nash equilibrium in game theory. By understanding this concept, we can analyze a wide range of strategic interactions and predict the outcomes of these interactions.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of dynamic games with incomplete information. We have explored the fundamental concepts and theories that govern these games, and how they differ from static games. We have also examined the role of information in these games, and how it can be used to strategically advantage players.

We have learned that dynamic games with incomplete information are characterized by the fact that players make decisions sequentially, and that each player's decisions can affect the decisions of subsequent players. This sequential decision-making process introduces a dynamic element to the game, which can lead to a variety of interesting and complex outcomes.

We have also discovered that incomplete information can be a powerful tool in these games. By strategically withholding information, players can create uncertainty and manipulate the decisions of their opponents. This can lead to a variety of interesting strategic interactions, and can greatly influence the outcome of the game.

In conclusion, dynamic games with incomplete information are a rich and complex field of study. They offer a wealth of opportunities for strategic decision-making and manipulation, and can lead to a variety of interesting and unpredictable outcomes. By understanding the theories and concepts presented in this chapter, you will be well-equipped to analyze and predict the outcomes of these games.

Consider a dynamic game with incomplete information. Suppose that player A makes a decision, and then player B makes a decision. Suppose also that player B has incomplete information about player A's decision. What strategies can player B use to maximize their payoff in this game?

Suppose also that player A has incomplete information about player B's decision, and that player B has incomplete information about player A's decision. What strategies can both players use to maximize their payoff in this game?

Suppose also that both players have incomplete information about each other's decisions, and that both players can strategically withhold information to manipulate the decisions of their opponents. What strategies can both players use to maximize their payoff in this game?

## Chapter: Chapter 5: Dynamic Games with Complete Information:

### Introduction

In the realm of microeconomic theory, the study of dynamic games with complete information is a crucial aspect that helps us understand the strategic interactions between rational agents over time. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of these games, providing a comprehensive guide to their analysis and application in various economic scenarios.

Dynamic games with complete information are a type of game where all players have complete information about the game, including the strategies and payoffs of all other players. This is in contrast to dynamic games with incomplete information, where players may have private information about their own strategies and payoffs. The complete information setting allows us to analyze the game in a more straightforward manner, as we can assume that all players have perfect information about the game and can make optimal decisions based on this information.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts and theories related to dynamic games with complete information. We will start by discussing the basic structure of these games, including the number of players, the strategies available to each player, and the payoff function that determines the outcome of the game. We will then move on to more advanced topics, such as the Nash equilibrium, the subgame perfect equilibrium, and the role of information in these games.

We will also discuss various applications of dynamic games with complete information in microeconomic theory. These applications include bargaining games, auctions, and repeated games, among others. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the theory and applications of dynamic games with complete information, and be equipped with the necessary tools to analyze these games in your own research or practice.

So, let's embark on this journey into the fascinating world of dynamic games with complete information, where strategic thinking, rational decision-making, and the interplay of information shape the outcomes of economic interactions over time.




### Subsection: 4.2a Definition of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of Bayesian Nash equilibrium and its applications in game theory. In this section, we will delve deeper into the concept of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE), a refinement of Bayesian Nash equilibrium.

#### 4.2a Definition of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) is a solution concept in game theory that describes the outcome of a turn-based game with incomplete information. It is a refinement of Bayesian Nash equilibrium, which is used for non-turn-based games. PBE uses Bayesian updating to describe player behavior in dynamic games with incomplete information.

In a PBE, each player's strategy and beliefs must satisfy certain conditions. The strategies and beliefs are determined by the following components:

1. Strategies: These are the possible actions that a player can take in the game.
2. Beliefs: These are the probabilities that a player assigns to the different types of their opponent.

The strategies and beliefs must satisfy the following conditions for a PBE to exist:

1. Rationality: Each player's strategy must be a best response to their beliefs about the other players' types.
2. Consistency: The beliefs about the other players' types must be consistent with the strategies chosen by the players.
3. Uniqueness: There can only be one PBE for a given game.

A PBE is always a Nash equilibrium. This means that no player can increase their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

#### 4.2a.1 Examples of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

To better understand the concept of PBE, let's consider an example. Consider the following game:

| Player's Payoff Matrix |
| --- | --- |
| Confess | Remain Silent |
| Confess | 10 | 10 |
| Remain Silent | 0 | 0 |

In this game, the strategy profile (Confess, Confess) is a PBE. The strategies and beliefs of the players are as follows:

1. Strategies: Confess and Remain Silent.
2. Beliefs: Each player believes that the other player will confess with probability 1.

This PBE satisfies the conditions of rationality, consistency, and uniqueness. The strategies and beliefs are also consistent with the payoff matrix.

In the next section, we will explore some applications of PBE in game theory.

### Subsection: 4.2b Properties of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

In the previous section, we defined the concept of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) and provided an example to illustrate its application. In this section, we will explore some of the key properties of PBE.

#### 4.2b.1 Uniqueness

As mentioned earlier, a PBE is a unique solution to a game. This means that there can only be one PBE for a given game. This property is a direct result of the consistency condition in the definition of PBE. If there were multiple PBEs, it would mean that there are multiple sets of strategies and beliefs that satisfy the conditions of PBE. However, the consistency condition ensures that there can only be one such set.

#### 4.2b.2 Rationality

The rationality condition in the definition of PBE ensures that each player's strategy is a best response to their beliefs about the other players' types. This means that each player is making the best decision given their beliefs about the other players. This property is crucial in ensuring that the PBE is a stable solution to the game.

#### 4.2b.3 Consistency

The consistency condition in the definition of PBE ensures that the beliefs about the other players' types are consistent with the strategies chosen by the players. This means that the players' beliefs about the other players' types are not contradictory. This property is essential in ensuring that the PBE is a feasible solution to the game.

#### 4.2b.4 Existence

The existence of a PBE is not always guaranteed. In some games, there may not be a PBE due to the complexity of the game or the lack of information about the other players' types. However, in many games, a PBE does exist and is a useful tool for analyzing the outcome of the game.

#### 4.2b.5 Robustness

The robustness of a PBE refers to its stability in the face of small deviations from the PBE. In other words, if a player deviates slightly from the PBE, will the PBE still be a stable solution? This property is important in real-world applications, where players may not always follow the PBE perfectly.

In the next section, we will explore some examples of PBEs to further illustrate these properties.

### Subsection: 4.2c Applications in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore some applications of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) in game theory. These applications will help us understand the practical implications of PBE and its role in decision-making.

#### 4.2c.1 Market Equilibrium Computation

One of the most common applications of PBE is in the computation of market equilibrium. In a market, buyers and sellers interact to determine the price and quantity of a good. The market equilibrium is the point at which the quantity demanded by buyers equals the quantity supplied by sellers.

The PBE can be used to compute the market equilibrium by considering the strategies and beliefs of the buyers and sellers. The strategies of the buyers and sellers are the prices at which they are willing to buy and sell the good, respectively. The beliefs of the buyers and sellers are their beliefs about the other players' strategies.

The PBE ensures that the market equilibrium is a stable solution, as each player's strategy is a best response to their beliefs about the other players' strategies. This means that the market equilibrium is not affected by small deviations from the PBE, making it a robust solution.

#### 4.2c.2 Contract Bridge

Another application of PBE is in the game of contract bridge. In this game, four players form two partnerships and compete against each other. Each player holds a hand of cards, and the goal is to win the most tricks.

The PBE can be used to analyze the strategies and beliefs of the players in this game. The strategies of the players are the bids they make to determine the trump suit and the contract. The beliefs of the players are their beliefs about the other players' cards and strategies.

The PBE ensures that the outcome of the game is a stable solution, as each player's bid is a best response to their beliefs about the other players' cards and strategies. This means that the outcome of the game is not affected by small deviations from the PBE, making it a robust solution.

#### 4.2c.3 Dynamic Games with Incomplete Information

PBE is particularly useful in dynamic games with incomplete information, where players make decisions sequentially and do not have complete information about the other players' types. In these games, PBE provides a way to analyze the strategies and beliefs of the players and determine the outcome of the game.

The PBE ensures that the outcome of the game is a stable solution, as each player's strategy is a best response to their beliefs about the other players' types. This means that the outcome of the game is not affected by small deviations from the PBE, making it a robust solution.

In conclusion, PBE is a powerful tool in game theory, with applications in various fields such as market equilibrium computation, contract bridge, and dynamic games with incomplete information. Its properties of uniqueness, rationality, consistency, existence, and robustness make it a valuable concept for understanding the outcome of games.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of dynamic games with incomplete information. We have explored the fundamental concepts and principles that govern these games, and how they differ from static games. We have also examined the role of information in these games, and how it can be used to strategically advantage players.

We have learned that dynamic games with incomplete information are characterized by the fact that players make decisions sequentially, and that each player only has partial information about the game state and the strategies of other players. This makes these games inherently more complex and strategic than static games, as players must not only consider their own decisions, but also anticipate the decisions of other players.

We have also seen how these games can be modeled and solved using various techniques, such as backward induction and Bayesian Nash equilibrium. These techniques provide a framework for understanding the strategic interactions between players, and for predicting the outcomes of these games.

In conclusion, dynamic games with incomplete information are a rich and complex field of study, with many interesting and important applications. By understanding the principles and techniques discussed in this chapter, we can gain a deeper understanding of strategic decision-making in a wide range of contexts.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 1
Consider a dynamic game with incomplete information where two players, A and B, must decide whether to invest in a risky project. Player A goes first, and can either invest or not invest. If A invests, B can either invest or not invest. If both invest, they each receive a payoff of 10. If both do not invest, they each receive a payoff of 5. If A invests and B does not, A receives a payoff of 8 and B receives a payoff of 2. If A does not invest and B invests, A receives a payoff of 3 and B receives a payoff of 7. Use backward induction to find the subgame perfect Nash equilibrium of this game.

#### Exercise 2
Consider a dynamic game with incomplete information where three players, A, B, and C, must decide whether to invest in a risky project. Player A goes first, and can either invest or not invest. If A invests, B and C can either invest or not invest. If all three invest, they each receive a payoff of 15. If two invest and one does not, they each receive a payoff of 10. If one invests and two do not, they each receive a payoff of 5. If no one invests, they each receive a payoff of 0. Use backward induction to find the subgame perfect Nash equilibrium of this game.

Consider a dynamic game with incomplete information where two players, A and B, must decide whether to invest in a risky project. Player A goes first, and can either invest or not invest. If A invests and B does not, A receives a payoff of 8 and B receives a payoff of 2. If A does not invest and B invests, A receives a payoff of 3 and B receives a payoff of 7. Use Bayesian Nash equilibrium to find the Nash equilibrium of this game.

## Chapter: Chapter 5: Advanced Topics in Game Theory

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 5 of "Microeconomic Foundations of Behavioral Economics: A Comprehensive Guide". This chapter delves into the advanced topics of game theory, a crucial component of behavioral economics. Game theory is a mathematical framework used to analyze decision-making in situations where the outcome of one's choices depends on the choices of others. It is a powerful tool that allows us to understand strategic interactions between rational and irrational agents.

In this chapter, we will explore the intricacies of game theory, building upon the foundational concepts introduced in earlier chapters. We will delve into the complexities of multi-player games, where the strategies of each player can influence the outcome of the game. We will also discuss the role of information in games, and how it can affect the strategies of players.

We will also explore the concept of Nash equilibrium, a fundamental solution concept in game theory. Nash equilibrium is a state in which no player can improve their outcome by unilaterally changing their strategy. It is named after mathematician John Nash, who first introduced the concept.

Furthermore, we will discuss the role of game theory in behavioral economics. Behavioral economics is a field that combines insights from psychology and economics to understand how people make decisions. Game theory provides a framework for understanding strategic decision-making, which is a key aspect of behavioral economics.

This chapter will provide a comprehensive understanding of advanced topics in game theory, equipping you with the necessary tools to analyze strategic interactions in a variety of scenarios. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practitioner in the field of economics, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource for understanding the complexities of game theory.

So, let's embark on this journey of exploring advanced topics in game theory, and unravel the strategic interactions that govern our economic world.




#### 4.2b Examples of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) and its components. In this section, we will explore some examples of PBE to further understand this concept.

##### Example 1: Gift Game 1

Consider the following game:

| Player's Payoff Matrix |
| --- | --- |
| Give | Not Give |
| Friend | 1 | 0 |
| Enemy | -1 | -1 |

In this game, the strategy profile (Give, Give) is a PBE. The strategies and beliefs of the players are as follows:

1. Strategies: Give and Not Give.
2. Beliefs: The sender believes that the receiver is a friend with probability p and an enemy with probability 1-p. The receiver believes that the sender is a friend with probability p and an enemy with probability 1-p.

This PBE exists for any value of p, but it is perverse if p ≥ .5. This means that the receiver's beliefs are not rational, as they would refuse any gift from the sender even if the sender is very likely to be a friend. This shows how PBE can lead to outcomes that are not Pareto efficient.

##### Example 2: Gift Game 2

Consider the following game:

| Player's Payoff Matrix |
| --- | --- |
| Give | Not Give |
| Friend | 1 | 0 |
| Enemy | -1 | -1 |

In this game, the strategy profile (Give, Give) is a PBE. The strategies and beliefs of the players are as follows:

1. Strategies: Give and Not Give.
2. Beliefs: The sender believes that the receiver is a friend with probability p and an enemy with probability 1-p. The receiver believes that the sender is a friend with probability p and an enemy with probability 1-p.

This PBE exists for any value of p, but it is perverse if p ≥ .5. This means that the receiver's beliefs are not rational, as they would refuse any gift from the sender even if the sender is very likely to be a friend. This shows how PBE can lead to outcomes that are not Pareto efficient.

##### Example 3: Gift Game 3

Consider the following game:

| Player's Payoff Matrix |
| --- | --- |
| Give | Not Give |
| Friend | 1 | 0 |
| Enemy | -1 | -1 |

In this game, the strategy profile (Give, Give) is a PBE. The strategies and beliefs of the players are as follows:

1. Strategies: Give and Not Give.
2. Beliefs: The sender believes that the receiver is a friend with probability p and an enemy with probability 1-p. The receiver believes that the sender is a friend with probability p and an enemy with probability 1-p.

This PBE exists for any value of p, but it is perverse if p ≥ .5. This means that the receiver's beliefs are not rational, as they would refuse any gift from the sender even if the sender is very likely to be a friend. This shows how PBE can lead to outcomes that are not Pareto efficient.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the concept of dynamic games with incomplete information. We have seen how these games differ from static games and how they can be used to model real-world situations where players' actions and information are not simultaneous. We have also learned about the different types of equilibria that can exist in these games, including subgame perfect equilibria and perfect Bayesian equilibria.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of understanding the role of information in dynamic games. Incomplete information can greatly impact the outcome of a game and can lead to different equilibria than what would be expected in a game with complete information. It is crucial for players to carefully consider the information they have and the information they can infer from their opponents' actions in order to make strategic decisions.

Another important concept we have explored is the idea of sequential equilibrium. This concept allows us to analyze dynamic games by breaking them down into smaller subgames and finding equilibria for each subgame. By combining these equilibria, we can determine the overall equilibrium for the entire game. This approach is particularly useful in complex games where finding a single equilibrium may be difficult.

Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive guide to understanding dynamic games with incomplete information. By studying these games, we can gain valuable insights into real-world situations and make more informed strategic decisions.

The revelation principle is a fundamental concept in mechanism design that helps us understand the relationship between a mechanism and its underlying game. It states that any mechanism that is incentive compatible can be simplified to a direct mechanism. This principle is crucial in designing mechanisms that are efficient and fair.

#### 4.2c Application in Game Theory

##### Application of PBE in Game Theory

Game theory is a mathematical framework used to analyze decision-making in situations where the outcome of one's choices depends on the choices of others. PBE is a key concept in game theory, as it provides a solution to dynamic games with incomplete information. It allows us to understand how rational agents make decisions in situations where they have limited information about the actions and preferences of other agents.

One of the main applications of PBE in game theory is in the analysis of bargaining games. In these games, two or more agents have to reach an agreement about the allocation of resources. The strategies and beliefs of the agents are often incomplete, and the outcome of the game depends on the actions and beliefs of all agents. PBE provides a solution to these games, by identifying the strategies and beliefs that are consistent with the agents' rationality.

Another important application of PBE is in the analysis of auctions. In these games, agents compete to buy a good or service. The strategies and beliefs of the agents are often incomplete, and the outcome of the game depends on the actions and beliefs of all agents. PBE provides a solution to these games, by identifying the strategies and beliefs that are consistent with the agents' rationality.

PBE also has applications in other areas of game theory, such as voting games, contract bridge, and the prisoner's dilemma. In all these games, PBE provides a solution by identifying the strategies and beliefs that are consistent with the agents' rationality.

In conclusion, PBE is a powerful tool in game theory, providing a solution to dynamic games with incomplete information. Its applications are vast and diverse, making it an essential concept for anyone studying advanced game theory and mechanism design.
